The Spiritual Influence of al-Hasan Abrak
Sanusi's attachment to al-Hasan Abrak and his praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deep spiritual connection between Imam al-Sanusi and the saint al-Hasan Abrak, and the significance of their relationship.

Core Idea
A saint's prayer and a scholar's humility forge a blessed bond.

This passage introduces one of the most important spiritual mentors in Imam al-Sanusi's life: al-Hasan Abrak. It describes how Sanusi regarded him as unparalleled among the saints and how Abrak reciprocated with a prophetic prayer. Reading this story helps us appreciate the role of spiritual guides in Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Notice the names and the key phrases. Then read the English translation to grasp the full meaning.
Pay attention to the Berber meaning of Abrak.
2
Identify the relationship
The passage shows a mutual respect. Note what Sanusi said about Abrak and what Abrak said to Sanusi. Think about what this tells us about their characters.
3
Reflect on the fulfillment
Ibn Maryam testifies that Abrak's prayer was fulfilled. Consider how this connects to the idea of spiritual insight (firasa) and the power of a saint's supplication.
This is a key point about the efficacy of righteous prayers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن النفوس الشريفة التي اتصلت أنفاس إمامنا السنوسي بها. الإمام الفقيه والقطب الكبير الحسن بن مخلوف بن مسعود المزيلي الراشدي، شهر بأبركان، ومعناه بلسان البربرية: الأسود، فلازمه كثيراً وانتفع به، وكان يقول: (رأيت المشايخ والأولياء، فما رأيت مثل سيدي الحسن أبركان)، وكان إذا دخل عليه الإمام السنوسي تبسم له، وفاتحه بالكلام، وقال له: (جعلك الله الأئمة من المتقين)، قال العلامة ابن مريم: (فحقق الله فراسته ودعوته فيه).
And among the noble souls to which the breaths of our Imam al-Sanusi were connected: the imam, jurist, and great pole al-Hasan ibn Makhluf ibn Mas'ud al-Muzili al-Rashidi, famous as Abrak, which in Berber means 'the black'; he accompanied him much and benefited from him. He (Sanusi) used to say: 'I have seen the shaykhs and the saints, but I have not seen anyone like my master al-Hasan Abrak.' And when Imam al-Sanusi entered upon him, he would smile at him and initiate conversation, and said to him: 'May God make you among the imams of the God-fearing.' The scholar Ibn Maryam said: 'God fulfilled his insight and his supplication in him.'
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage describes one of the most important spiritual influences on Sanusi: al-Hasan Abrak. It defines Abrak's identity and gives the meaning of his name in Berber. It then records Sanusi's statement that he never saw a saint like him, showing his high esteem. It also notes that Abrak greeted Sanusi with a prayer that he would be among the imams of the God-fearing, which Ibn Maryam testifies was fulfilled.

Key Terms

أبركان (Abrak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'Abrak' means 'blessed' or has a positive connotation.
Correction: The text explicitly states that 'Abrak' in Berber means 'the black' (الأسود), a simple physical description, not a spiritual title.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Childhood Sign of a Pure Gold Piece
Childhood recognition and Sufi dedication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early recognition of Sanusi's spiritual potential through a childhood sign and his dedication to the Sufi path.

Core Idea
A single childhood gesture marked Sanusi as a pure gold piece of spiritual potential.

This passage recounts a telling childhood incident where Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid recognized Sanusi's innate purity. It also describes how Sanusi's heart was drawn to Sufism and his earnest pursuit of its path.

Guided Path
1
Read the childhood incident
Read the Arabic passage describing the encounter between young Sanusi and Ibn Marzuq. Notice the specific gesture and words used.
The phrase 'نقرة خالصة' is a metaphor meaning 'pure gold piece'.
2
Reflect on the sign's significance
Consider why Ibn Marzuq's action is highlighted. What does it suggest about Sanusi's nature?
3
Understand Sanusi's Sufi path
Read the description of Sanusi's love for Sufism and his view of the path as one of action, not just words. Reflect on how this connects to the childhood sign.
The phrase 'وهي ليست بطريق قال، بل جد ومثابرة وفعال' emphasizes action over speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي صغره كان إذا مرَّ مع الصبيان على الإمام ابن مرزوق الحفيد . . وضع يديه على رأسه وقال: نقرة خالصة! ويظهر أنَّ محبة الصوفية قد تَبَلَتْ فؤادَهُ، فسعى سعياً حثيثاً للريادة في طريقهم، وهي ليست بطريقِ قال، بل جد ومثابرة وفعال، ولذا تجد الشكاية بطي بساطها، حتى قال الإمام السنوسي نفسه: (من الغرائب في زماننا هذا أن يوجد عالم جُمع له علم الظاهر والباطن على أكمل وجه؛ بحيث يُنتفع به في العلمين، فوجود مثله في غاية الندور، فمن وجده فقد وجد كنزاً عظيماً دنيا وأخرى، فليشدَّ عليه يده؛ لئلا يضيع عن قرب فلا يجد مثله شرقاً وغرباً أبداً) به عقب على هذه الكلمة الشيخ الملالي تلميذ الإمام: (وكأنه أشار خطف لنفسه، فلم يلبث بعده حتى ولا شك أنه لا يوجد مثله أبداً) فكأنه كاشفنا بذلك،
And in his childhood, when he passed with the boys by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid, he placed his hands on his head and said: 'A pure gold piece!' And it appears that love of Sufism had saturated his heart, so he strove strenuously for leadership in their path, and it is not a path of speech, but of seriousness, perseverance, and action, and therefore you find complaint laying aside its carpet, until Imam al-Sanusi himself said: 'Among the strange things in this time of ours is that there might be a scholar who has gathered for him the knowledge of the exoteric and esoteric in the most complete manner, such that he is benefited from in both sciences; his existence is extremely rare. Whoever finds him has found a great treasure, worldly and next-worldly, so let him hold on to him firmly, lest he lose him soon and never find his like east or west ever again.' The shaykh al-Mallali, the student of the Imam, commented on this statement: 'It is as if he pointed to himself, for after him there did not remain, and there is no doubt, that his like will ever exist.' So it is as if he disclosed that to us.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents two indications of Sanusi's spiritual distinction. First, an early childhood incident: when he passed by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid, the Imam placed his hands on Sanusi's head and called him 'a pure gold piece' (naqra khalisah), recognizing his innate purity. Second, the author observes that love of Sufism filled Sanusi's heart, leading him to strive in their path—which is not merely verbal but requires seriousness and action. This culminates in Sanusi's own statement about the extreme rarity of a scholar who masters both exoteric and esoteric sciences, calling such a person a great treasure. The author then notes that al-Mallali, a student of Sanusi, commented that Sanusi seemed to be referring to himself, implying that after Sanusi no one like him would exist. Thus the passage shows how Sanusi's unusual qualities were recognized early and later confirmed by his own words and his disciple's interpretation.

Key Terms
نقرة خالصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's spiritual potential was recognized early through a simple, powerful gesture.

Have you ever received a compliment or recognition of your potential? Did that lead you to strive harder, or did you become complacent? What will you do today to turn any recognition you've received into action?
?
The passage says Sufism is not just talk but action. In what area of your worship or character have you been content with words and intentions without actual effort? What specific step will you take this week to move from saying to doing?
?
Ibn Marzuq saw something pure in young Sanusi. Do you believe that Allah has placed pure potential in you? What is one area where you are not living up to that potential because you've settled for less?

Sanusi on the Rarity of the Perfect Scholar
Sanusi's statement on rare scholars and its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's own statement on the extreme rarity of a scholar who masters both exoteric and esoteric sciences.

Core Idea
Sanusi considered a master of both exoteric and esoteric knowledge a treasure almost impossible to find.

In this passage, Sanusi makes a remarkable statement about the rarity of a complete scholar. His disciple al-Mallali then interprets it as a reference to Sanusi himself, suggesting that Sanusi was that rare treasure.

Guided Path
1
Read Sanusi's statement
Read the Arabic text of Sanusi's statement carefully. Note the qualities he describes: mastery of both exoteric (ظاهر) and esoteric (باطن) sciences.
The phrase 'جُمع له علم الظاهر والباطن' means he has gathered both types of knowledge.
2
Understand the rarity and value
Sanusi says such a scholar's existence is 'extremely rare' and calls him 'a great treasure'. Reflect on why he emphasizes holding onto him firmly.
The treasure is described as worldly and otherworldly.
3
Consider al-Mallali's commentary
Read al-Mallali's comment that Sanusi was secretly referring to himself. Why would the disciple interpret it that way?
The phrase 'وكأنه أشار خطف لنفسه' means 'as if he pointed quickly to himself'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي صغره كان إذا مرَّ مع الصبيان على الإمام ابن مرزوق الحفيد . . وضع يديه على رأسه وقال: نقرة خالصة! ويظهر أنَّ محبة الصوفية قد تَبَلَتْ فؤادَهُ، فسعى سعياً حثيثاً للريادة في طريقهم، وهي ليست بطريقِ قال، بل جد ومثابرة وفعال، ولذا تجد الشكاية بطي بساطها، حتى قال الإمام السنوسي نفسه: (من الغرائب في زماننا هذا أن يوجد عالم جُمع له علم الظاهر والباطن على أكمل وجه؛ بحيث يُنتفع به في العلمين، فوجود مثله في غاية الندور، فمن وجده فقد وجد كنزاً عظيماً دنيا وأخرى، فليشدَّ عليه يده؛ لئلا يضيع عن قرب فلا يجد مثله شرقاً وغرباً أبداً) به عقب على هذه الكلمة الشيخ الملالي تلميذ الإمام: (وكأنه أشار خطف لنفسه، فلم يلبث بعده حتى ولا شك أنه لا يوجد مثله أبداً) فكأنه كاشفنا بذلك،
And in his childhood, when he passed with the boys by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid, he placed his hands on his head and said: 'A pure gold piece!' And it appears that love of Sufism had saturated his heart, so he strove strenuously for leadership in their path, and it is not a path of speech, but of seriousness, perseverance, and action, and therefore you find complaint laying aside its carpet, until Imam al-Sanusi himself said: 'Among the strange things in this time of ours is that there might be a scholar who has gathered for him the knowledge of the exoteric and esoteric in the most complete manner, such that he is benefited from in both sciences; his existence is extremely rare. Whoever finds him has found a great treasure, worldly and next-worldly, so let him hold on to him firmly, lest he lose him soon and never find his like east or west ever again.' The shaykh al-Mallali, the student of the Imam, commented on this statement: 'It is as if he pointed to himself, for after him there did not remain, and there is no doubt, that his like will ever exist.' So it is as if he disclosed that to us.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents two indications of Sanusi's spiritual distinction. First, an early childhood incident: when he passed by Imam Ibn Marzuq al-Hafid, the Imam placed his hands on Sanusi's head and called him 'a pure gold piece' (naqra khalisah), recognizing his innate purity. Second, the author observes that love of Sufism filled Sanusi's heart, leading him to strive in their path—which is not merely verbal but requires seriousness and action. This culminates in Sanusi's own statement about the extreme rarity of a scholar who masters both exoteric and esoteric sciences, calling such a person a great treasure. The author then notes that al-Mallali, a student of Sanusi, commented that Sanusi seemed to be referring to himself, implying that after Sanusi no one like him would exist. Thus the passage shows how Sanusi's unusual qualities were recognized early and later confirmed by his own words and his disciple's interpretation.

Key Terms
علم الظاهر والباطن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi's statement is a general maxim without personal reference.
Correction: Al-Mallali's commentary makes clear that Sanusi was pointing to himself, as the context and his disciple's interpretation show.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's statement, what should one do upon finding such a rare scholar?
A) Learn from him briefly.
B) Hold on to him firmly.
C) Publicize his location.
D) Follow him only in esoteric matters.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's statement about the rare scholar, interpreted by his disciple, points to Sanusi himself as that unique treasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi’s disciple recognized that he was a treasure beyond compare. Look at the scholars and teachers in your life right now – do you truly value them as irreplaceable gifts, or have you become indifferent to their presence? What would it take for you to ‘hold on to them firmly’ before they are gone?
?
The passage says once lost, such a treasure is never found again. Is there a specific person from whom you have gained spiritual or religious knowledge whom you have neglected or taken for granted? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to express your gratitude to them.
?
If you believe that no one after Sanusi can match his combination of knowledge, does that challenge you to lower your expectations of contemporary scholars? How does that affect your responsibility to seek knowledge from whoever is available, even if imperfect? Are you using the rarity of perfect scholars as an excuse to stop learning?

Sanusi's Teachers and the Introduction of Kalam to the Maghreb
Al-Shafshawani lists Sanusi's teachers and highlights al-Ayli's role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly lineage of Imam Sanusi and the historical role of al-Ayli in bringing the science of kalam to the Maghreb.

Core Idea
Sanusi's teachers link him to the first transmitter of kalam in the Maghreb: al-Ayli.

This passage from al-Shafshawani provides a window into the educational network that shaped Imam Sanusi. It not only lists his teachers but also highlights al-Ayli as a pivotal figure who first introduced kalam to the Maghreb in later times. Understanding this lineage helps us appreciate the chains of transmission that preserved and spread Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the teachers
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note each teacher's name and any descriptor given (e.g., 'commentator of al-Burda').
Al-Ayli is given special attention.
2
Focus on al-Ayli's role
Notice the phrase about al-Ayli being the first to introduce kalam to the Maghreb in later times. Consider why this is significant for the history of Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the chain of transmission
Note that al-Shafshawani attributes this information to his own teacher, showing the importance of isnad (chain of transmission) in scholarly traditions.
This connects to the broader concept of ijaza and scholarly lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال العلامة الشفشاوني: (وأشياخه وأشياخ ابن زكري واحد، ومن أشياخهما العالم الرحال الأيلي، بسكون اللام وفتح الهمزة وضم الياء وكسر اللام، وهو أول من أدخل علم الكلام إلى المغرب في الأزمنة المتأخرة، والشيخ ابن مرزوق شارح «البردة»، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن العباس شارح «لامية ابن مالك»، والشيخ أبو العباس أحمد بن زاغ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله أقرقار، والشيخ أبو عثمان قاسم العقباني، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن الجلاب، أفادني بذلك شيخنا أبو عبد الله محمد شقرون بن هبة الله).
And the scholar al-Shafshawani said: 'His shaykhs and the shaykhs of Ibn Zakari are the same, and among their shaykhs is the traveling scholar al-Ayli (with sukun on lam, fath on hamza, damm on ya, kasr on lam), and he is the first to introduce the science of kalam into the Maghreb in later times; and Shaykh Ibn Marzuq, commentator of al-Burda; and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Abbas, commentator of the Lamiyya of Ibn Malik; and Shaykh Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Zaghu; and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah Aqrar [or Aqrqar]; and Shaykh Abu Uthman Qasim al-Uqbani; and Shaykh Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Jallab. My shaykh Abu Abd Allah Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah informed me of that.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from al-Shafshawani, providing a list of the teachers of Imam Sanusi. It first notes that Sanusi's teachers were the same as those of another scholar named Ibn Zakari. Then it names them, beginning with al-Ayli, described as a traveling scholar and important for being the first to introduce the science of kalam (dialectical theology) to the Maghreb in later times. The other teachers listed are: Ibn Marzuq, commentator of the Burda poem; Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Abbas, commentator of Ibn Malik's Lamiyya; Ahmad ibn Zaghu; Abu Abd Allah Aqrar; Qasim al-Uqbani; and Abu Abd Allah ibn al-Jallab. The source of this information is given as al-Shafshawani's own teacher, Abu Abd Allah Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah. This passage demonstrates the scholarly lineage of Sanusi and highlights the importance of al-Ayli in transmitting kalam to the region.

Key Terms
علم الكلام (kalam)
الأيلي (al-Ayli)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi's teachers included al-Ayli, who is credited with introducing kalam to the Maghreb in later times, connecting Sanusi to the broader tradition of Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Sanusi on the Rarity of Combining Zahir and Batin Knowledge
Sanusi describes the rarity of a scholar combining outward and inward knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's emphasis on the rarity of a scholar who masters both outward (zahir) and inward (batin) knowledge, and the importance of preserving such scholars.

Core Idea
A scholar who combines outward and inward knowledge is a rare treasure to be held onto.

This passage reveals Sanusi's deep connection to Sufism and his lament for the scarcity of scholars who excel in both exoteric and esoteric sciences. It teaches us to value and hold onto such exceptional individuals, as they are a great blessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the translation to understand the meaning. Note the emotional tone of Sanusi's words.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms 'zahir' (outward) and 'batin' (inward) knowledge. Understand that Sanusi is talking about a scholar who has both perfectly.
These two categories of knowledge are central in Islamic scholarship.
3
Understand Sanusi's advice
Sanusi urges whoever finds such a scholar to hold fast to him, lest he be lost. Reflect on why he gives this urgent advice.
The rarity makes it imperative to seize the opportunity.
4
Consider al-Mallali's commentary
Al-Mallali interprets Sanusi's words as pointing to himself, implying that Sanusi himself was that rare scholar. Notice how this adds a layer of meaning.
This shows the high regard in which Sanusi was held.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويظهر أنَّ محبة الصوفية قد تَبَلَتْ فؤادَهُ ، فسعى سعياً حثيثاً للريادة في طريقهم ، وهي ليست بطريقِ قال ، بل جد ومثابرة وفعال ، ولذا تجد الشكاية بطي بساطها ، حتى قال الإمام السنوسي نفسه : ( من الغرائب في زماننا هذا أن يوجد عالم جُمع له علم الظاهر والباطن على أكمل وجه ؛ بحيث يُنتفع به في العلمين ، فوجود مثله في غاية الندور ، فمن وجده فقد وجد كنزاً عظيماً دنيا وأخرى ، فليشدَّ عليه يده ؛ لئلا يضيع عن قرب فلا يجد مثله شرقاً وغرباً أبداً ) به عقب على هذه الكلمة الشيخ الملالي تلميذ الإمام : ( وكأنه أشار خطف لنفسه ، فلم يلبث بعده حتى ولا شك أنه لا يوجد مثله أبداً ((۱) فكأنه كاشفنا بذلك ،
It appears that love of the Sufis had taken hold of his heart, so he strove eagerly for leadership in their path, and it is not a path of mere speech, but of seriousness, perseverance, and action. Therefore you find the complaint spread out, until Imam al-Sanusi himself said: 'Among the wonders of this our time is that there exists a scholar who has been given both the outward and inward knowledge in the most complete manner, such that benefit is derived from him in both sciences. The existence of such a person is extremely rare, and whoever finds him has found a great treasure in this world and the next. So let him hold fast to him, lest he be lost soon, and he will never find his like in the East or the West ever again.' The Shaykh al-Mallali, a student of the Imam, commented on this statement: 'It is as if he indicated that he would be taken away, for he did not remain long after that, and there is no doubt that no one like him will ever exist.' So it is as if he disclosed that to us.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative describing Imam al-Sanusi's deep engagement with Sufism and his concern for the preservation of comprehensive scholarship. It begins with the author's observation that al-Sanusi's love for the Sufis had taken possession of his heart, prompting him to strive earnestly for leadership in their way—emphasizing that this path is one of serious effort and action, not merely words. The passage then cites a statement from al-Sanusi himself, where he marvels at the rarity of a scholar who has mastered both exoteric (zahir) and esoteric (batin) sciences perfectly, so that people benefit from him in both fields. Al-Sanusi urges whoever finds such a person to hold onto him tightly, warning that he may soon be lost and never be replaced. The passage then records a comment by his student al-Mallali, who interprets al-Sanusi's words as an allusion to his own imminent death—indeed al-Mallali states that al-Sanusi did not live long after saying this, and that no equal to him would ever appear again. The passage thus serves as both a testimony to al-Sanusi's own rare excellence and a lament for the loss of such comprehensive scholars.

Key Terms
محبة الصوفية
علم الظاهر
علم الباطن
الندور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Sanusi's statement is a general rule about all scholars.
Correction: The statement is a specific lament about the extreme rarity of those who combine both types of knowledge perfectly, not a general classification.

Al-Ayli and the Teachers of Imam al-Sanusi
Al-Shafshawni lists Sanusi's teachers and notes al-Ayli's pioneering role in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of al-Ayli in introducing kalam to the Maghreb and the scholarly network that shaped Imam al-Sanusi's education.

Core Idea
Al-Ayli brought kalam to the Maghreb in later times, linking Sanusi to a broader intellectual tradition.

Al-Shafshawni provides a valuable window into the intellectual lineage of Imam al-Sanusi. This passage lists his teachers, highlighting al-Ayli as the key figure who reintroduced the science of kalam to the Maghreb. By tracing these teachers, we see how Sanusi was connected to the wider Islamic scholarly world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the names and the flow of the text.
The passage is a quotation from al-Shafshawni.
2
Identify al-Ayli's role
Find the sentence about al-Ayli. He is described as the first to introduce kalam to the Maghreb in later times.
The phrase 'in later times' is important—it implies a revival, not absolute priority.
3
List the other teachers
Count the names after al-Ayli. They are all teachers of al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri.
The list includes Ibn Marzuq, Ibn al-Abbas, Ibn Zag, Aqraqar, al-Uqbani, and Ibn al-Jallab.
4
Understand the chain of transmission
Notice that al-Shafshawni cites his own teacher as the source. This shows the importance of isnad in Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال العلامة الشفشاوني : ( وأشياخه وأشياخ ابن زكري واحد ، ومن أشياخهما العالم الرحال الأيلي ، بسكون اللام وفتح الهمزة وضم الياء وكسر اللام ، وهو أول من أدخل علم الكلام إلى المغرب في الأزمنة المتأخرة ، والشيخ ابن مرزوق شارح « البردة » ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن العباس شارح » لامية ابن مالك » ، والشيخ أبو العباس أحمد بن زاغ ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله أقرقار ، والشيخ أبو عثمان قاسم العقباني ، والشيخ أبو عبد الله بن الجلاب ، أفادني بذلك شيخنا أبو عبد الله محمد شقرون بن هبة الله )(۲)
And the scholar al-Shafshawni said: 'His teachers and the teachers of Ibn Zakri are one and the same. Among their teachers is the traveler scholar al-Ayli—with sukun on the lam, fatha on the hamza, damma on the ya, and kasra on the lam—and he is the first to introduce the science of kalam into the Maghreb in later times. And Shaykh Ibn Marzuq, commentator on al-Burda; and Shaykh Abu Abdullah ibn al-Abbas, commentator on the Lamiyya of Ibn Malik; and Shaykh Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Zag; and Shaykh Abu Abdullah Aqraqar; and Shaykh Abu Uthman Qasim al-Uqbani; and Shaykh Abu Abdullah ibn al-Jallab. My teacher Abu Abdullah Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah informed me of that.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from the scholar al-Shafshawni, providing a list of the teachers of both Imam al-Sanusi and another scholar named Ibn Zakri, indicating they shared the same masters. It highlights the figure of al-Ayli, a traveling scholar whose name is pointed with full diacritics for correct pronunciation (sukun on lam, fatha on hamza, damma on ya, kasra on lam). Al-Shafshawni notes that al-Ayli was the first to introduce the science of kalam (ilm al-kalam, rational theology) to the Maghreb in later periods—making him a key transmitter of this discipline. He then enumerates several other teachers: Ibn Marzuq (commentator on the Burda poem), Abu Abdullah ibn al-Abbas (commentator on Ibn Malik's Lamiyya), Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Zag, Abu Abdullah Aqraqar, Abu Uthman Qasim al-Uqbani, and Abu Abdullah ibn al-Jallab. The passage ends with al-Shafshawni citing his own source for this information: his teacher Muhammad Shaqrun ibn Hibat Allah. The passage thus functions as a chain of transmission for the teachers of al-Sanusi, underscoring the scholarly networks of the time.

Key Terms
أشياخ
علم الكلام
المغرب
الأزمنة المتأخرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ayli was the first person ever to introduce kalam to the Maghreb.
Correction: Al-Shafshawni specifies 'in later times' (al-azmina al-muta'akhkhira), meaning he revived or reintroduced it after an earlier tradition had faded.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ayli is credited with reintroducing kalam to the Maghreb in later times, and the list of teachers shows the rich scholarly network that shaped Imam al-Sanusi's education.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Sanusi's Students and the Curious Omission of al-Mallali
Al-Tanbakti names notable students of al-Sanusi and the author notes al-Mallali did not write a chapter on al-Sanusi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the wide scholarly influence of al-Sanusi through his students and the notable absence of a dedicated biography by his disciple al-Mallali.

Core Idea
A teacher's legacy is seen in his students, but even a devoted historian may not write a separate chapter on his master.

Al-Tanbakti's list of al-Sanusi's students shows the breadth of his teaching influence across North Africa and beyond. Yet, the author points out that one of his foremost students, al-Mallali, who compiled his life's details, did not write a separate chapter on him. This lesson examines these two pieces of historical information and what they reveal about scholarly networks and biography.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the names and the author's observation sink in.
The passage has two parts: a list and a comment.
2
Identify the students listed
Scan the list of names: Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madin, etc. Notice the description 'other virtuous ones who took from the Imam' shows many more.
These are notable scholars who studied with al-Sanusi.
3
Focus on al-Mallali's omission
Read the second sentence about al-Mallali. He is called 'one of his foremost students, the historian of his life, and the collector of his reports and conditions.' Yet he did not write a separate chapter on al-Sanusi. Ask why that might be.
This is a curious point for historians.
4
Consider the broader significance
Think about how this passage shows the transmission of knowledge through students and the choices biographers make. The omission might be because al-Mallali integrated al-Sanusi's life into a larger work.
The common misunderstanding is that al-Mallali ignored al-Sanusi entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العلامة التنبكتي : ( أخذ عنه أعلام ؛ كابن صعد ، وأبي القاسم الزواوي ، وابن أبي مدين ، والشيخ يحيى بن محمد ، وابن الحاج البيدري ، وابن العباس الصغير ، وولي الله محمد القلعي ريحانة زمانه ، وإبراهيم الوجديجي ، وابن ملوكة ، وغيرهم من الفضلاء ((۱) الآخذين عن الإمام ) ولم يعقد الشيخ الملالي باباً للحديث عن السنوسي ، وهو واحد من أعيان تلامذته ، والمؤرخ لسيرته ، ومجمع أخباره وأحواله
The scholar al-Tanbakti said: 'Eminent figures studied under him, such as Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madin, Shaykh Yahya ibn Muhammad, Ibn al-Hajj al-Baydari, Ibn al-Abbas al-Saghir, Wali Allah Muhammad al-Qal'i—the fragrance of his time—Ibrahim al-Wujdiji, Ibn Muluka, and other virtuous ones who took from the Imam.' And Shaykh al-Mallali did not devote a chapter to discussing al-Sanusi, even though he was one of his foremost students, the historian of his life, and the collector of his reports and conditions.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage has two parts. First, it quotes al-Tanbakti listing a number of notable students who learned from Imam al-Sanusi: Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madin, Shaykh Yahya ibn Muhammad, Ibn al-Hajj al-Baydari, Ibn al-Abbas al-Saghir, Wali Allah Muhammad al-Qal'i (described as 'the fragrance of his time'), Ibrahim al-Wujdiji, Ibn Muluka, and other virtuous individuals who took knowledge from the Imam. The list demonstrates the wide influence of al-Sanusi across different regions and scholarly circles. The second part is the author's own observation: despite al-Mallali being one of al-Sanusi's most distinguished students—indeed, the historian of his life and the compiler of his reports and states—he did not write a separate chapter (bab) on al-Sanusi in his works. This is presented as a notable omission, perhaps indicating that al-Mallali's biographical coverage of his teacher was indirect or embedded in other writings. The passage thus combines a roster of students with a critical note on the absence of a dedicated biography by a key disciple.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that al-Mallali completely ignored al-Sanusi or was not a devoted student.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that al-Mallali was 'one of his foremost students, the historian of his life and the collector of his reports and conditions,' but simply did not dedicate a separate chapter to him.

Checkpoint

A) He wrote an extensive biography of him
B) He did not write a separate chapter on him despite being his student
C) He criticized al-Sanusi in his writings
D) He was the only student to record his teachings

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that al-Sanusi had many eminent students, and that even a devoted biographer like al-Mallali may not write a separate chapter on his teacher, leaving his biography embedded in larger works.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a teacher or mentor who has shaped your understanding of Islam. Have you ever taken the time to consciously preserve their teachings or recount their contributions to others, even if informally? What holds you back from doing so?
?
Al-Mallali compiled the life of his teacher but did not set aside a separate chapter for him. Are you more focused on building your own legacy or on honoring and transmitting the legacy of those who taught you? How can you shift your priorities today?
?

Imam Zarruq as a Student and Peer of al-Sanusi
Al-Sanusi's student Zarruq, a peer and notable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the teacher-student relationship between al-Sanusi and Ahmad Zarruq, and how Zarruq's peer status highlights al-Sanusi's scholarly authority.

Core Idea
Even a master like Zarruq was a student of al-Sanusi.

This brief passage introduces us to one of al-Sanusi's most notable students, Ahmad Zarruq. Though Zarruq was a peer in age and shared many teachers, he personally acknowledged studying under al-Sanusi. This reveals the depth of al-Sanusi's influence and the respect he commanded among his contemporaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the description of Zarruq as both a student and a peer.
The phrase 'من جملة أقرانه' is key: it means 'among his peers.'
2
Identify the relationship
Focus on the phrase 'تتلمذ للإمام السنوسي' (studied under al-Sanusi). Despite being a peer, Zarruq is explicitly a student. Identify how the text emphasizes Zarruq's high rank while maintaining his student status.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does the author mention that Zarruq was a peer? How does this strengthen al-Sanusi's reputation? The commentary will help you answer.
This passage guards against the misunderstanding that being a student implies lower status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان ممن تتلمذ للإمام السنوسي وهو من جملة أقرانه ، وأعيان أهل زمانه . . الإمام الشيخ العارف بالله تعالى أحمد بن أحمد البرنسي الفاسي المعروف بـ ( زروق ) ، والمتوفى سنة ( ٨٩٩ هـ ) على جلالة شأنه وعلو قدره ، وقد شاركه في كثير من شيوخه ، وقد نص على التلمذة للإمام السنوسي بنفسه كما نقل ذلك المؤرخ العلامة التنبكتي
And among those who studied under Imam al-Sanusi, while being among his peers and the notables of his time, was the Imam, the knower of Allah, Ahmad ibn Ahmad al-Barnusi al-Fasi, known as Zarruq, who died in the year 899 AH, despite his lofty status and high rank. He shared many of his teachers with him, and he himself explicitly stated that he studied under Imam al-Sanusi, as reported by the historian and scholar al-Tanbakti.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage identifies Imam Ahmad Zarruq (d. 899 AH) as a student of Imam al-Sanusi, despite the fact that Zarruq was a contemporary and peer—both were among the notables of their time and shared many teachers. The author emphasizes Zarruq's high rank: he is described as 'the knower of Allah' (al-arif billah) and a man of great stature. Yet, despite his own eminence, Zarruq personally acknowledged that he studied under al-Sanusi. This is confirmed by the historian al-Tanbakti. The passage thus establishes a teacher-student relationship between two major scholars, underscoring al-Sanusi's influence even over those of comparable standing. It also provides a specific death year for Zarruq (899 AH), serving as a chronological anchor.

Key Terms

أقرانه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zarruq's high rank and peer status imply he did not study under al-Sanusi or that it would diminish his own standing.
Correction: The passage explicitly states Zarruq studied under al-Sanusi and personally affirmed it. His peer status actually highlights al-Sanusi's superiority.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Ahmad Zarruq, despite his own high rank and peer status, studied under al-Sanusi, demonstrating al-Sanusi's profound scholarly influence.

Life of Imam al-Sanusi

Students and Teaching Sessions of Imam al-Sanusi
Tanbakti lists Sanusi's prominent students and al-Balawi describes learning sessions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly lineage and teaching methods of Imam al-Sanusi as described by his students and biographers.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sanusi’s students and teaching sessions reveal a vibrant culture of scholarly transmission.

This passage introduces us to the renowned students of Imam al-Sanusi and offers a rare eyewitness account of his teaching sessions. Through the reports of al-Tanbakti and al-Balawi, we glimpse the devotional atmosphere and rigorous scholarly traditions surrounding one of the great theologians of North Africa.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of students
Begin by reading the names of al-Sanusi's students cited by al-Tanbakti. Notice how the author emphasizes that al-Mallali did not write a chapter on Sanusi despite being a prominent student.
2
Focus on the description of teaching sessions
Al-Balawi describes a typical session: crowded with students and commoners, Quran recitation with seven qira'at, and commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari. Identify the key elements of these sessions.
3
Reflect on the ritual recitation of 'Aqida al-Sughra'
The Friday Asr ritual where students recited the creed from memory in unison while Sanusi engaged in dhikr. Consider how this reflects the integration of creed and practice.
This shows the reverence for his theological work.
4
Consider al-Balawi's regret
Al-Balawi laments not being able to read directly to Sanusi due to his busy schedule. This highlights the high demand for learning from him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال العلامة التنبكتي : ( أخذ عنه أعلام ؛ كابن صعد ، وأبي القاسم الزواوي ، وابن أبي مدين ، والشيخ يحيى بن محمد ، وابن الحاج البيدري ، وابن العباس الصغير ، وولي الله محمد القلعي ريحانة زمانه ، وإبراهيم الوجديجي ، وابن ملوكة ، وغيرهم من الفضلاء ((۱)
الآخذين عن الإمام
ولم يعقد الشيخ الملالي باباً للحديث عن السنوسي ، وهو واحد من أعيان تلامذته ، والمؤرخ لسيرته ، ومجمع أخباره وأحواله .
وكان ممن تتلمذ للإمام السنوسي وهو من جملة أقرانه ، وأعيان أهل زمانه . . الإمام الشيخ العارف بالله تعالى أحمد بن أحمد البرنسي الفاسي المعروف بـ ( زروق ) ، والمتوفى سنة ( ٨٩٩ هـ ) على جلالة شأنه وعلو قدره ، وقد شاركه في كثير من شيوخه ، وقد نص على التلمذة للإمام السنوسي بنفسه كما نقل ذلك المؤرخ العلامة التنبكتي (۲)
ومن جملة الأعلام الذين اجتمعوا به ، وحضروا عموم مجالسه دون القراءة عليه مباشرةً : أبو جعفر أحمد بن علي البلوي الوادي آشي المتوفى سنة ( ٩٣٨ هـ ) ، وقد أرَّخَ له ولسيرته العطرة في « ثبته » المشهور ، ومن جملة ما قاله : ( لقيته رضي الله تعالى عنه ، وحضرت مجلسه الغاص بالمستفيدين من طلبة العلم والعامة بمسجده قرب داره بدرب مسوفة من داخل تلمسان أمَّنَها الله تعالى ، وحضرت الفاتحة » وأوائل سورة ( البقرة » تقرأ عليه بالسبع ، وكتباً غير ذلك ؛ منها ( البخاري » ، كان يُقرأ عليه في بعض مجالس حضرتها ويتكلّم على أحاديثه بالكلام الذي يدلُّ على مقامه في العلم والعبادة وغيره من كتب المجلس
وحضرنا - يومَ سلمنا عليه إثرَ ما صلينا العصر خلفه ـ « عقيدته الصغرى » تقرأ بين يديه ، يقرؤها طلبته وجمع من العوام الملازمين لمجلسه عن ظهر قلب سرداً على صوتٍ واحد إثر سلامِهِ من صلاة عصر يوم الجمعة عادة مستمرة ، وهو قاعد بمحرابه ، مقبل على الذكر )(۱) وقال : ( ولم تقدَّرْ لي القراءة عليه مع رغبتي في ذلك وحرصي عليه ؛ لاستغراق طلبته أوقات قعوده ، حتى إنهم كانوا يقرؤون عليه والرملية في يد أحدهم إذا فرغت قطع ، وكنت أؤمل القراءة وأترصد لها وقتاً ، فعاجلته - قدَّسَهُ الله تعالى - المَنِيَّةُ ، ولم أنل من الأمنية ((٢)
The scholar al-Tanbakti said: 'Prominent figures studied under him, such as Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madin, Shaykh Yahya ibn Muhammad, Ibn al-Hajj al-Baydari, Ibn al-Abbas al-Saghir, Wali Allah Muhammad al-Qala'i (the fragrant herb of his time), Ibrahim al-Wajdiji, Ibn Muluka, and other virtuous persons.' (1) Those who studied under the Imam. And Shaykh al-Mallali did not dedicate a chapter to speaking about al-Sanusi, although he is one of the foremost of his students, the historian of his biography, and the compiler of his news and circumstances. And among those who studied under Imam al-Sanusi while being among his peers and the eminent of his time is the Imam, the gnostic of Allah Almighty, Ahmad ibn Ahmad al-Barnusi al-Fasi known as (Zarruq), who died in the year 899 AH, despite his great status and high rank, and he shared many of his teachers [with Sanusi], and he explicitly stated that he studied under Imam al-Sanusi himself, as reported by the historian al-Tanbakti (2). And among the eminent figures who gathered with him and attended his general sessions without reading directly under him: Abu Ja'far Ahmad ibn Ali al-Balawi al-Wadi Ashi, who died in the year 938 AH, and he recorded his life and fragrant biography in his famous 'Thabt', and among what he said: 'I met him—may Allah be pleased with him—and attended his session crowded with seekers of knowledge and the common people in his mosque near his house in Darb Masufa inside Tlemcen, may Allah protect it, and I attended the recitation of al-Fatiha and the beginnings of Surat al-Baqara read to him with the seven recitations, and other books, including al-Bukhari, which was read to him in some sessions I attended, and he would comment on its hadiths with speech indicating his station in knowledge and worship, and other books of the session. And we attended—on the day we greeted him after we prayed Asr behind him—his 'Aqida al-Sughra' being read before him; his students and a group of common people who were constant in his session recited it from memory, in unison, following his greeting from the Asr prayer on Friday, as a continuous habit, while he sat in his prayer niche, engaged in dhikr.' (1) And he said: 'I was not able to read to him despite my desire and eagerness for that, because his students occupied all his sitting times, such that they would read to him and a sandglass was in the hand of one of them; when it emptied, they would stop. I hoped for reading and watched for an opportunity, but death—may Allah sanctify him—hastened to him, and I did not attain the wish.' (2)
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account from two scholars, al-Tanbakti and al-Balawi, about the students and teaching methods of Imam al-Sanusi. It begins by listing eminent students who studied directly under Imam al-Sanusi, including Ibn Sa'd, Abu al-Qasim al-Zawawi, Ibn Abi Madin, and others. Then it notes that al-Mallali, a historian, did not write a chapter on al-Sanusi despite being his student. Next, it mentions Ahmad Zarruq as another prominent student who was also a peer of Sanusi, sharing several teachers. Then it focuses on Abu Ja'far al-Balawi, who attended Sanusi's sessions but did not read directly under him. Al-Balawi provides a vivid eyewitness description: he attended Sanusi's crowded sessions in his mosque at Tlemcen, where the Quran was recited with the seven qira'at, and Sahih al-Bukhari was read with commentary. He describes a special custom after the Asr prayer on Friday: Sanusi's students and common followers would recite his 'Aqida al-Sughra' from memory in unison while he remained in his prayer niche engaged in dhikr. Al-Balawi laments that he could not personally read to Sanusi because students filled all his time, and he died before finding an opportunity. This passage highlights both the scholarly lineage and the devotional atmosphere around Sanusi's teaching.

Key Terms

تلامذة
عقيدته الصغرى
ثبت
أبو جعفر البلوي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam al-Sanusi's teaching was only for advanced scholars and not open to the general public.
Correction: Al-Balawi notes that the session was crowded with 'seekers of knowledge and the common people', and the Friday ritual included both his students and 'a group of common people who were constant in his session.'

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi's teaching sessions combined rigorous scholarship with communal devotion, as seen in the Friday ritual recitation of his 'Aqida al-Sughra'.

?

Al-Sanusi's Teaching Sessions and the Recitation of the Aqida al-Sughra
Al-Balawi describes attending al-Sanusi's sessions and the communal recitation of the Aqida al-Sughra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional educational setting in which al-Sanusi taught his 'Small Creed' and how it was recited communally by students and laypeople alike.

Core Idea
The Aqida al-Sughra was recited by heart by commoners after Friday Asr prayer.

This passage gives us a rare glimpse into the actual teaching practice of Imam al-Sanusi. It is not a theological argument but a biographical account from a student who attended his gatherings. Through this anecdote, we see how the Aqida al-Sughra was embedded in the spiritual life of the community, recited collectively from memory after the Asr prayer on Fridays.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical context
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the setting: al-Balawi describes meeting al-Sanusi and attending his sessions.
Notice that the passage is from al-Balawi's 'Thabat', a collection of his teachers.
2
Identify the key practices described
Look for the specific activities: recitation of the Fatiha and Baqara in seven modes, reading of Bukhari, and the Friday Asr recitation of the Aqida al-Sughra.
These practices show the integration of Qur'an, hadith, and creed in the same gathering.
3
Note the role of the 'Small Creed'
Observe that the Aqida al-Sughra was recited from memory by both students and commoners in unison, indicating it was a core text for the community.
This counters the idea that the creed was only for advanced scholars.
4
Reflect on al-Balawi's regret
Consider al-Balawi's sadness at not being able to read directly with al-Sanusi due to students monopolizing his time, highlighting the value placed on direct transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القراءة عليه مباشرةً : أبو جعفر أحمد بن علي البلوي الوادي آشي المتوفى سنة ( ٩٣٨ هـ ) ، وقد أرَّخَ له ولسيرته العطرة في « ثبته » المشهور ، ومن جملة ما قاله : ( لقيته رضي الله تعالى عنه ، وحضرت مجلسه الغاص بالمستفيدين من طلبة العلم والعامة بمسجده قرب داره بدرب مسوفة من داخل تلمسان أمَّنَها الله تعالى ، وحضرت الفاتحة » وأوائل سورة ( البقرة » تقرأ عليه بالسبع ، وكتباً غير ذلك ؛ منها ( البخاري » ، كان يُقرأ عليه في بعض مجالس حضرتها ويتكلّم على أحاديثه بالكلام الذي يدلُّ على مقامه في العلم والعبادة وغيره من كتب المجلس وحضرنا - يومَ سلمنا عليه إثرَ ما صلينا العصر خلفه ـ « عقيدته الصغرى » تقرأ بين يديه ، يقرؤها طلبته وجمع من العوام الملازمين لمجلسه عن ظهر قلب سرداً على صوتٍ واحد إثر سلامِهِ من صلاة عصر يوم الجمعة عادة مستمرة ، وهو قاعد بمحرابه ، مقبل على الذكر )(۱) وقال : ( ولم تقدَّرْ لي القراءة عليه مع رغبتي في ذلك وحرصي عليه ؛ لاستغراق طلبته أوقات قعوده ، حتى إنهم كانوا يقرؤون عليه والرملية في يد أحدهم إذا فرغت قطع ، وكنت أؤمل القراءة وأترصد لها وقتاً ، فعاجلته - قدَّسَهُ الله تعالى - المَنِيَّةُ ، ولم أنل من الأمنية ((٢)
Reading upon him directly: Abu Ja'far Ahmad ibn 'Ali al-Balawi al-Wadi Ashi, who died in the year 938 AH, and he chronicled him and his fragrant biography in his famous 'Thabat', and among what he said: 'I met him, may Allah be pleased with him, and attended his gathering crowded with beneficiaries from students of knowledge and commoners in his mosque near his house in Darb Masufa inside Tlemcen, may Allah protect it, and I attended the Fatiha and the beginnings of Surat al-Baqara recited to him in the seven modes, and other books; among them al-Bukhari, which was read to him in some gatherings I attended, and he spoke on its hadiths with speech indicating his station in knowledge and worship, and other books of the gathering. And we attended - on the day we greeted him after having prayed Asr behind him - his 'Small Creed' read before him, his students and a group of commoners who regularly attended his gathering reciting it from memory in unison on one voice after his greeting from the Asr prayer on Friday, a continuous custom, while he sat in his prayer niche, engaged in remembrance.' (1) And he said: 'I was not destined to read upon him despite my desire and eagerness for that, because his students occupied all his sitting times, to the point that they would read to him and the pen was in the hand of one of them; when it finished, it was cut. I hoped to read and waited for a time, but death hastened to him - may Allah sanctify him - and I did not attain the wish.' (2)
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a biographical anecdote from al-Balawi's 'Thabat' describing his encounter with Imam al-Sanusi. It begins by identifying the student (al-Balawi) and his work. Then it quotes al-Balawi's description: he attended al-Sanusi's gathering, which was crowded with students and commoners. The Fatiha and early verses of Surat al-Baqara were recited to him in the seven modes of recitation, and other books like al-Bukhari were read, with al-Sanusi commenting on hadiths. A notable practice is described: on a Friday after Asr prayer, al-Sanusi's 'Small Creed' (al-Aqida al-Sughra) was recited from memory by students and commoners in unison as a regular custom, while al-Sanusi sat in his prayer niche engaged in dhikr. Finally, al-Balawi expresses regret that he was unable to read directly with al-Sanusi because students monopolized his time; he hoped for an opportunity but al-Sanusi died before he could.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commoners in this account knew the Aqida al-Sughra by heart and recited it weekly after Asr prayer. Do you know the essential beliefs of your faith by heart? What is stopping you from memorizing a concise creed like the Aqida al-Sughra and making it part of your regular worship?
?
Al-Sanusi sat in his prayer niche engaged in dhikr while his students and commoners recited the creed. After your prayers, do you remain in dhikr, or do you rush to your daily affairs? What specific change will you make this week to increase your post-prayer remembrance of Allah?
?
Al-Balawi deeply regretted missing the opportunity to read directly with al-Sanusi because the students occupied all his time. Are you making full use of the scholars and teachers available to you today? If you were to die tomorrow, would you have any regrets about not learning more from those who can teach you?

Al-Sanusi's Three Early Works
Enumeration of al-Sanusi's three early works with descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Sanusi's three early works and their distinguishing features, including the unexpected subject of his first book.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's first work was on inheritance law, not theology.

Al-Sanusi's student al-Mallali compiled a list of his teacher's writings. In this passage, he introduces the three foundational works that shaped al-Sanusi's early career, including a surprising fact: his first book was not on theology, but on inheritance law. Let's explore each work and its unique details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to grasp the overall structure and content.
Note the three numbered items in the passage.
2
Identify the three works
List the three works in order as presented. Observe that the first is on inheritance law, the second on theology, and the third a commentary.
3
Examine the details of each work
For the first work, note the author's age and the story with the shaykh. For the second, note its description as 'solid and difficult'. For the third, note its extensive nature.
The story of hiding the first work until age 40 is a unique historical detail.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider how this passage corrects the common assumption that al-Sanusi's first work was theological. Why is this distinction important?
It shows the breadth of his early scholarship.

His writings and [something] world. The compositions of Imam al-Sanusi varied in the Islamic library, and he is one of those who authored much. Despite this diversity, his doctrinal compositions are the crown of his knowledge. And his student al-Shaykh al-Mallali devoted a special chapter to discussing them. Commentary
This passage is a classified listing of Imam al-Sanusi's early works. It begins by noting the diversity of his writings and that his doctrinal works are the crown of his scholarly output. His student al-Mallali wrote a special chapter on these works. The passage then enumerates three works: (1) 'al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi', which is his first composition, written at around age 18-19. It notes a disagreement about his exact age and mentions a story with a shaykh who ordered him to hide it until he turned forty. (2) 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-Aqida al-Kubra', described as his first work in the science of tawhid, with a solid and difficult text as stated in his own commentary on the 'Aqida al-Wusta'. (3) 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra', which is one of his most extensive doctrinal books. The passage thus provides a brief overview of his foundational writings.

Catalogue of Sanusi's Major Theological Works
Al-Mallali enumerates Sanusi's major theological writings with descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range and significance of Imam al-Sanusi's theological writings as enumerated by his student al-Mallali.

Core Idea
Sanusi's theological works form a ladder from beginner to advanced, each with a distinct purpose.

Imam al-Sanusi is known for his concise creed, but his theological output was vast and carefully graded. In this passage, his student al-Mallali catalogues the major works, describing their content, fame, and relation to each other. This overview helps us appreciate the depth and systematic nature of Sanusi's contribution to Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the list of works.
Notice the descriptive titles and the order from earliest to latest.
2
Identify each work and its description

3
Understand the grading system
Notice the terms 'al-Kubra', 'al-Wusta', 'al-Sughra', and 'Sughra al-Sughra'. These indicate levels of size and difficulty.
This grading is deliberate: each creed is for a different audience.
4
Appreciate the singularity of al-Sughra
Reflect on why al-Mallali says the Small Creed (Umm al-Barahin) is unequaled by any creed before or after.

His works and the works of the world? The compositions of Imam al-Sanusi varied in the Islamic library, and he is one of those who wrote extensively. Despite this variety, his theological writings were the crown of his learning. His student al-Shaykh al-Mallali dedicated a special chapter to discussing them, and you will see before you all the compositions he mentioned; they are:
—
:
(1) 'al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi': it is his first composition, and his age when he wrote it was nineteen or eighteen years, with some uncertainty about that, and his story with al-Shaykh al-‘Arif bi-Allah al-Hasan Abarkan and his order to him to hide it until he reaches the age of forty will come.
- 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-‘Aqida al-Kubra' (3): it is the first thing he composed in the science of tawhid, and its expression is firm and difficult as stated in 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Wusta' (4).
- 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Kubra': this commentary is considered one of the most extensive theological books that the Imam authored.
(1) See 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' (folio 102).
(2) See p. 45.
(3) Thus al-Imam al-Sanusi himself used to name it, and see p. 151. As for the addition in its original title: '(al-Mukharraja min Zulumat al-Jahl wa Rabiqat al-Taqlid...)' etc., it is a description of it, and not part of its name as both al-‘Akari and al-Hamidi see, with al-Mallali's affirmation of it.
(4) See p. 123.
22

- 'al-‘Aqida al-Wusta': it is briefer than 'al-Kubra' and above 'al-Sughra'.
- 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Wusta': it is among the gems authored in the science of tawhid.
- 'al-‘Aqida al-Sughra' (known as 'Umm al-Barahin' and 'Dhat al-Barahin' and 'al-Sanusiyya al-Sughra'): it is the pearl of his creeds, and what is meant by their saying: 'the creed of al-Sanusi' when unqualified. It achieved a fame that no other achieved, and scholars turned to it for commentary, and students for memorization and study. Al-‘Allama al-Mallali said: (It is among the greatest creeds, and no creed from those who came before or after equals it, and the Shaykh, may Allah be pleased with him, alluded to that in the opening of his commentary on it) (1).
- 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Sughra', also known as 'Tawhid Ahl al-‘Irfan wa Ma'rifat Allah wa Rusulihi bi al-Dalil wa al-Burhan', and as 'Sharh Umm al-Barahin'. It too achieved widespread fame and dissemination across horizons, and how many glosses were written upon it.
- 'Sughra al-Sughra' (2): al-Shaykh al-Mallali described it saying: (his concise creed in extreme brevity, and it is smaller than 'al-‘Aqida al-Sughra' as you will see).
(1) See 'al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' (folio 103).
(2) It is sometimes called 'al-‘Aqida al-Wajiza', and thus also called 'Sughra Sughra al-Sughra' as you will see.
23
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a bibliographical enumeration by al-Mallali of Imam al-Sanusi's major theological works. It begins by noting that despite the variety of al-Sanusi's writings, his theological works were the crown ('taj al-‘ilmi') of his output. Al-Mallali dedicated a special chapter to listing them.

The first work is 'al-Muqrib al-Mustawfi fi Sharh Fara'id al-Hawfi', a commentary on a legal work by al-Hawfi, written when al-Sanusi was about 18-19 years old. A story is mentioned about al-Shaykh al-Hasan Abarkan ordering him to hide this work until he reached forty.

Next is 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' or 'al-‘Aqida al-Kubra', his first work in theology ('tawhid'), described as having firm and difficult expression. Then comes 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid' or 'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Kubra', an extensive commentary on the previous work.

Then 'al-‘Aqida al-Wusta' is described as intermediate between 'al-Kubra' and 'al-Sughra'. Its commentary ('Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Wusta') is praised as a gem.

The famous 'al-‘Aqida al-Sughra', known as 'Umm al-Barahin' (Mother of Proofs), 'Dhat al-Barahin', and 'al-Sanusiyya al-Sughra', is called the pearl of his creeds. It achieved unmatched fame, with many commentaries and study. Al-Mallali declares it unequaled by any creed before or after.

'Sharh al-‘Aqida al-Sughra' (also 'Tawhid Ahl al-‘Irfan' or 'Sharh Umm al-Barahin') also gained widespread fame and many glosses.

Finally, 'Sughra al-Sughra' (sometimes 'al-‘Aqida al-Wajiza') is an ultra-concise creed, smaller than 'al-Sughra'.

العقيدة الكبرى
عمدة أهل التوفيق والتسديد
العقيدة الوسطى
العقيدة الصغرى
أم البراهين
السنوسية الصغرى
شرح العقيدة الصغرى
صغرى الصغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam al-Sanusi only authored one creed, the 'Small Creed' (Umm al-Barahin).
Correction: The passage shows he authored multiple works of varying lengths, including al-Kubra, al-Wusta, al-Sughra, and Sughra al-Sughra, as well as commentaries and a non-theological work.

Checkpoint

B) المقرب المستوفي في شرح فرائض الحوفي
C) العقيدة الصغرى
D) العقيدة الوسطى

Key Takeaway
Al-Mallali's catalogue reveals that Sanusi produced a graded series of theological works, from the extensive al-Kubra to the ultra-concise Sughra al-Sughra, with the Small Creed (Umm al-Barahin) being the most famous and widely studied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If someone asked you for a single book on aqeedah, would you even know which level to recommend? Or do you avoid the responsibility of guiding others because you haven't done the work yourself? Let that question unsettle you until you take action.

Listing the poem and three early works

This lesson introduces you to a catalog of works associated with Imam al-Sanusi. You will see how his writings span theology, hadith, and logic, and how scholars like al-Mallali evaluated them. The beginning of his book with his saying: (It is a poem containing the two ways of guidance for the elite and the common people), and it is a poem in the meter of al-basīṭ, and it is also known as al-Jazā’iriyya in the beliefs of faith, and the learned poet is the one who asked Imam al-Sanūsī to comment on it. Mukammal Ikmal al-Ikmal (by Imam al-Abī: it is a commentary on Sahih Imam Muslim), said al-Shaykh al-Mallālī: (He added in it strange subtleties and amazing pearls, and it is in two large volumes)(2). - Commentary on Sahih al-Imam al-Bukhari: al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: (He did not complete it), and he mentioned that he reached (the chapter on ‘He who avoids doubtful things for his religion’)(3). - Commentary on the Difficulties of Sahih al-Bukhari: these similar hadiths occur at its end, and they are problematic for one of narrow understanding and weak expression. - Abridgment of al-Zarkashī’s Commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari: al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: (I saw it in his handwriting)(4). - Abridgment of al-Taftāzānī’s Gloss on al-Kashshāf: perhaps it is a nice abridgment; since ‘al-Sa‘d’s Gloss on al-Kashshāf’ was not completed. - Commentary on al-Yāsamīniyya: al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: (Among them is his commentary which he wrote on the ‘Introduction to Algebra’ by Abū Muḥammad ‘Abd Allāh ibn Ḥajjāj known as Ibn al-Yāsamīn), and he wrote this commentary in the time of his youth, in his handwriting (1), and I saw it. - Commentary on al-Khūnajī’s Compendium in logic: perhaps he did not complete it; al-Mallālī said: (I saw some notebooks of it)(2). - Commentary on Īsāghūjī in logic: al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: (It is by Abū al-Ḥasan Ibrāhīm ibn ‘Umar ibn al-Ḥasan al-Rubbāṭ ibn ‘Alī ibn Abī [Bakr] al-Biqā‘ī al-Shāfi‘ī, and it is a large commentary, vast in knowledge)(3). - Commentary on the Abridgment of Imam Ibn ‘Arafa al-Warghamī in logic: he solved therein what was difficult of the expression of Imam Ibn ‘Arafa; al-Shaykh al-Mallālī said: (Shaykh Rāḍī Allāh ‘anhu told me: Ibn ‘Arafa’s speech is very difficult, especially in this ‘Abridgment’ (4), and he used to seclude himself to solve his expressions, and he did not complete it. Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and bibliographical enumeration of works and commentaries related to Imam al-Sanūsī. It begins by describing a poem that contains two ways of guidance, known as al-Jazā’iriyya on Islamic beliefs, written in the basīṭ meter. The poet requested al-Sanūsī to write a commentary on it, which al-Balawī praises as precious and filled with benefits. Then follows a numbered list of other works, likely continuing from a previous page, including: (1) Mukammal Ikmal al-Ikmal by al-Abī, a commentary on Sahih Muslim, which al-Mallālī says contains strange subtleties in two volumes. (2) A commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari that was left incomplete, reaching only a specific chapter. (3) A commentary on the difficult hadiths of Sahih al-Bukhari. (4) An abridgment of al-Zarkashī’s commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari, seen by al-Mallālī. (5) An abridgment of al-Taftāzānī’s gloss on al-Kashshāf, noted as nice but possibly linked to the incomplete gloss of al-Sa‘d. (6) A commentary on al-Yāsamīniyya, which al-Mallālī says was written in the author’s youth. (7) A commentary on al-Khūnajī’s Compendium in logic, possibly incomplete, with only a few notebooks seen. (8) A commentary on Īsāghūjī in logic, authored by al-Biqā‘ī, described as large and vast. (9) A commentary on Ibn ‘Arafa’s Abridgment in logic, where the author solved difficult expressions with seclusion but did not complete it. The passage ends with footnote references for each item. This list serves as a record of the scholarly output and interactions of al-Sanūsī with various works.

Key Terms
الجزائرية
مكمل إكمال الإكمال
شرح مشکلات صحيح البخاري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All listed works are authored solely by al-Sanusi.
Correction: Some works are by other scholars, and al-Sanusi may have commented on or abridged them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which work did al-Sanusi not complete?
A) Mukammal Ikmal al-Ikmal
B) Sharh Sahih al-Bukhari
C) Sharh Mushkilat Sahih al-Bukhari
D) The poem al-Jazairiyya

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's scholarly contributions include both original commentaries and abridgments of others' works, often left incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Sanusi left his commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari incomplete. What unfinished work of knowledge or good deed are you leaving behind that you need to complete or resume today?
?
The poem al-Jazairiyya was sent to al-Sanusi from a distant land, and he accepted the task despite his other commitments. When was the last time you took on a difficult task asked of you, especially one that would benefit others, without delay or complaint?
?
Al-Sanusi's commentary on the difficult hadiths was meant for those of 'weak understanding.' Do you humbly admit your own weaknesses in understanding your religion, or do you pretend knowledge you do not have? Decide one step you will take this week to remedy an area of ignorance in your faith.

Abridgments and a Youthful Commentary
Listing three abridgments and one youthful commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the works of al-Sanusi that are abridgments of earlier commentaries, including his youthful work on algebra.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's abridgments make complex works accessible.

This lesson covers three more entries from the list of al-Sanusi's works: abridgments of al-Zarkashi's commentary on Bukhari, al-Taftazani's gloss on al-Kashshaf, and a commentary on an algebraic introduction. Each reflects his skill in condensing and clarifying.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that these are described as abridgments or short works.
2
Identify each work
List the three works: Mukhtasar Sharh al-Zarkashi, Mukhtasar Hashiyat al-Taftazani, and Sharh al-Yasminiyya.
The third is a commentary on a mathematical text.
3
Note the details
Pay attention to al-Mallali's remarks: he saw the first in Sanusi's handwriting, the second is a nice abridgment, and the third was written in youth.
This shows Sanusi's early scholarly activity.

This passage is a biographical and bibliographical enumeration of works and commentaries related to Imam al-Sanūsī. ?

Logic Commentaries by Sanusi
Listing three logic commentaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sanusi's works on logic, including his commentaries on al-Khunaji, Isaghuji, and Ibn Arafa.

Core Idea
Logic was a key part of Sanusi's scholarly output, though some works remain incomplete.

This lesson presents the final group of works in the list: three commentaries on logic texts. They highlight al-Sanusi's engagement with the logical tradition and the difficulties he faced in explaining complex works like Ibn Arafa's abridgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that two of these commentaries were possibly incomplete.
2
Identify each work
List: Sharh Jumal al-Khunaji, Sharh Isaghuji, and Sharh Mukhtasar Ibn Arafa.
All are in logic.
3
Note the comments
Al-Mallali says he saw only notebooks of the first; the second is by another author; and the third was difficult and left incomplete.
This shows the collaborative nature of scholarship.

Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and bibliographical enumeration of works and commentaries related to Imam al-Sanūsī. It begins by describing a poem that contains two ways of guidance, known as al-Jazā’iriyya on Islamic beliefs, written in the basīṭ meter. The poet requested al-Sanūsī to write a commentary on it, which al-Balawī praises as precious and filled with benefits. Then follows a numbered list of other works, likely continuing from a previous page, including: (1) Mukammal Ikmal al-Ikmal by al-Abī, a commentary on Sahih Muslim, which al-Mallālī says contains strange subtleties in two volumes. (2) A commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari that was left incomplete, reaching only a specific chapter. (3) A commentary on the difficult hadiths of Sahih al-Bukhari. (4) An abridgment of al-Zarkashī’s commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari, seen by al-Mallālī. (5) An abridgment of al-Taftāzānī’s gloss on al-Kashshāf, noted as nice but possibly linked to the incomplete gloss of al-Sa‘d. (6) A commentary on al-Yāsamīniyya, which al-Mallālī says was written in the author’s youth. (7) A commentary on al-Khūnajī’s Compendium in logic, possibly incomplete, with only a few notebooks seen. (8) A commentary on Īsāghūjī in logic, authored by al-Biqā‘ī, described as large and vast. (9) A commentary on Ibn ‘Arafa’s Abridgment in logic, where the author solved difficult expressions with seclusion but did not complete it. The passage ends with footnote references for each item. This list serves as a record of the scholarly output and interactions of al-Sanūsī with various works.

Misconception: All three commentaries were written by Sanusi himself.
Correction: The commentary on Isaghuji is by al-Biqai, not Sanusi.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult text, do you persevere like Sanusi did, secluding himself to solve the expressions, or do you give up? What is one hard topic you have avoided? Will you commit to spending focused time on it this week?
?
Sanusi left some works incomplete. Is there a project or study in your own life that you have started but not finished? What is it, and what step can you take today to move it forward?
?
Knowledge often requires humility—Sanusi studied a commentary by another scholar (al-Biqā‘ī). Are you willing to learn from others, even those you might consider less knowledgeable? Name one person from whom you can learn something new this week.

Catalog of the Author's Works and Their Status
Lists works by the author with notes on completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of works attributed to the author and their completion status as recorded by al-Mallāli.

Core Idea
The author's literary output spans logic, mathematics, astronomy, and fiqh, with many works left unfinished.

This lesson presents a catalog of works by the author (likely Sanusi) as reported by his biographer al-Mallāli. It offers a glimpse into the scholarly production of a North African scholar across multiple disciplines. The list also reveals which projects were completed and which were left unfinished.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disciplines covered
Read through the list and note which fields are represented: logic, mathematics, astronomy, Qur'anic readings, Maliki fiqh.
This catalog is a snapshot of the author's scholarly interests.
2
Focus on completion status
For each work, check whether al-Mallāli states it was completed, left incomplete, or status unknown.
Many entries explicitly say 'لم يكمله' (he did not complete it) or similar.
3
Notice the sources of information
Pay attention to phrases like 'قال الشيخ الملالي' (al-Shaykh al-Mallāli said) and 'رأيته' (I saw it). These indicate direct testimony.
Al-Mallāli is the primary source for these details.
4
Observe the author's own remarks
Look for comments in the author's own voice, such as on Ibn ʿArafa's difficulty and the need for seclusion.
These give insight into the author's working methods.
5
Reflect on scholarly priorities
Consider what this catalog tells us about the author's chosen fields and the state of incomplete projects.
Incompleteness was common; it does not diminish the value of what was accomplished.

Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a catalog of works by the author (likely Abū ʿAbd Allāh Muḥammad ibn Yūsuf al-Sanūsī, given the book context), as reported by al-Shaykh al-Mallāli. It lists several commentaries and original works, primarily in logic, mathematics, astronomy, Qur'anic readings, and Mālikī fiqh, noting in each case whether the work was completed or not. The passage begins with an epitome of al-Taftazānī's gloss on al-Kashshāf (a tafsīr work), which the author characterizes as a nice abridgement because the original gloss was never finished. Next is a commentary on al-Yāsimīniyya (an algebra text), written in the author's youth, which the compiler saw. Then come logic works: a commentary on al-Khūnajī's Jumal (perhaps incomplete), a commentary on Īsāghūjī (attributed to al-Biqāʿī, not the author), and a commentary on Ibn ʿArafa's epitome, which the author worked on in seclusion due to its difficulty but did not complete. Next is an epitome in logic with additions to al-Khūnajī's work, and a commentary on that epitome, which is said to suffice for the mutakallim. Then an astronomical work explaining an astrolabe text by the author's teacher. After that, a commentary on Ibn Sīnā's poem (unfinished), an epitome on the seven Qur'anic readings (unknown to al-Mallāli), a commentary on al-Shāṭibiyya (seen incomplete), and finally two Mālikī fiqh commentaries: one on al-Mudawwana (possibly incomplete) and one on al-Waghlīsiyya (only a small part done). The form of each entry is: title followed by a colon and a comment from al-Mallāli or the author's own remark.

Correction: Some works, such as the commentary on Īsāghūjī, are attributed by al-Mallāli to another scholar (al-Biqāʿī). The catalog includes works that the author commented on, not necessarily his own compositions.
