The Attribution of al-Ḥafīda to Imam Sanusi
Biographer discusses the uncertain attribution of a short creed to Sanusi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical uncertainty surrounding the attribution of the creed al-Ḥafīda to Imam al-Sanusi and the evidence scholars use to evaluate authorship.

Core Idea
Attribution of a text is not proven by popularity alone.

Before studying the theological content of a classic creed, it is important to critically assess its authorship. This lesson examines a short discussion from a biographer about the well-known creed al-Ḥafīda, attributed to Imam al-Sanusi. The passage highlights the cautious approach scholars take when definitive proof is lacking.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Familiarize yourself with the biographer's tone and the key points about attribution.
Notice the cautious language used, such as 'no definitive proof' and 'among what was attributed'.
2
Identify the two positions on attribution
The passage presents two main positions: (1) there is no definitive proof linking the creed to Sanusi, only reliance on later scholars like al-Ḥifnāwī; (2) the abundance of early commentaries and consistent wording may give confidence, but even a commentator like al-Shaktānī expressed uncertainty.
3
Reflect on scholarly methodology
Consider why the biographer includes al-Shaktānī's cautious statement. Reflect on how scholars balance evidence (commentaries, consistency) with the lack of explicit proof. This illustrates academic honesty in attribution.
The takeaway is that even well-known texts may have uncertain origins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه العناوين كلُّها لعقيدة لطيفة جداً ، لا تتجاوز الصفحتين سهلة العبارة ، واضحة الدلائل ، قريبة - كما وصفت - من جميع الأذهان على تفاوت فهومها ، ولكن لا مستند يقطع بنسبة هذه العقيدة المباركة المتفق على ما فيها إلى الإمام السنوسي ، بل النص المنقول إليك عن العلامة الحفناوي هو الذي دار عليه المتأخرون في إثباتها . نعم ؛ تعدد شروحها ، واتفاق عباراتها ، وتقدم هذه الشروح تاريخياً ؛ إذ يعود كثير منها إلى مطلع القرن الحادي عشر الهجري إلى منتصفه . . قد يجعلنا نثق بنسبتها إلى الإمام السنوسي ، على أن الإمام الشكتاني - وهو واحد من شرَّاحها - اكتفى بقوله : ( وكان من جملة ما نُسب إليه - يعني : الإمام السنوسي - العقيدة المسماة بـ «الحفيدة)) (۱). وهذه من الإمام الشكتاني كلمة إنصاف وتحقيق ، ولو كان بين يديه حينها ما يؤكد نسبتها إلى الإمام السنوسي . . لجزم وقطع بذلك لكنه اكتفى بهذه العبارة المشكّكة ، ولم يعبأ بهذا التشكيك مع حسن عبارة هذه العقيدة ووجازتها وقرب مأخذها (۱) انظر ( التحفة المفيدة » ( ص ٤٠ ) .
And all these titles belong to a very fine creed, not exceeding two pages, easy in expression, clear in evidence, close - as described - to all minds despite the variation of their understandings. But there is no definitive proof for the attribution of this blessed creed, whose contents are agreed upon, to Imam al-Sanusi. Rather, the text transmitted to you from al-‘Allāma al-Ḥifnāwī is what later scholars relied upon in affirming it. Yes, the multiplicity of its commentaries, the agreement of their expressions, and the historical precedence of these commentaries - many of them dating from the beginning of the eleventh century AH to its middle - may make us confident in its attribution to Imam al-Sanusi, although Imam al-Shaktānī - one of its commentators - sufficed with his statement: 'Among what was attributed to him - meaning Imam al-Sanusi - is the creed named al-Ḥafīda (1).' This from Imam al-Shaktānī is a word of fairness and verification. If he had had in his hands at that time what confirmed its attribution to Imam al-Sanusi, he would have affirmed and decisively stated it, but he sufficed with this doubtful expression, and he did not care about this doubt given the good expression of this creed, its conciseness, and its accessible source. (1) See al-Tuhfa al-Mufīda, p. 40.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a discussion by the biographer about the attribution of a short creed to Imam al-Sanusi. The biographer first describes the creed: it is very concise (two pages), easy in wording, clear in proofs, and accessible to all minds. However, there is no decisive evidence for its attribution to Sanusi. The text commonly used to support the attribution comes from al-Ḥifnāwī, and later scholars have relied on it. The biographer notes that the large number of commentaries on this creed, the consistency of their wording, and the fact that many commentaries date from the early 11th century AH to its middle, might give confidence that it is indeed by Sanusi. But then he mentions Imam al-Shaktānī, one of the commentators, who only said that the creed was 'among what was attributed to him'. Al-Shaktānī's phrasing is cautious and implies uncertainty; if he had definitive proof, he would have stated it categorically. The biographer concludes that despite this doubt, the creed's fine expression and conciseness are undeniable.

Key Terms
عقيدة
الحفيدة
الإمام السنوسي
شرح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The multiplicity of commentaries on al-Ḥafīda proves beyond doubt that it was authored by Imam al-Sanusi.
Correction: The biographer notes that while the number of commentaries and their consistency may inspire confidence, a definitive link is still lacking, as even a commentator like al-Shaktānī only attributed it to Sanusi with uncertainty.

Checkpoint
Why does the biographer express uncertainty about the attribution of the creed to Imam al-Sanusi?
A) Because the creed is too simple to be from al-Sanusi.
B) Because there is no definitive textual evidence linking it to him, only reliance on later scholars and cautious statements from commentators.
C) Because the creed was written by al-Ḥifnāwī.
D) Because the creed contradicts al-Sanusi's other works.

Key Takeaway
Despite its popularity and numerous commentaries, the creed al-Ḥafīda lacks definitive proof of authorship by Imam al-Sanusi, and scholarly caution remains appropriate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a religious practice or teaching is authentic simply because it is widely accepted? Where in your own life might you be clinging to something without verifying its source? Will you investigate it today?
?
Imagine you regularly recite a supplication of unknown origin, like al-Ḥafīda. Would its uncertain authorship shake your trust in it? How would you balance the benefit of its meaning with the need for intellectual honesty?
?
Al-Shaktānī remained cautious even when all signs pointed to authenticity. Is there an area of your faith where you are afraid to admit uncertainty? Will you embrace the discomfort for the sake of truth?

Scholarly Praise for Sanusi's Works
Al-Shafshawi and al-Hibti praise the precision and flawlessness of Sanusi's works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high scholarly esteem for Sanusi's writings and the specific qualities praised by al-Shafshāwī and al-Hibṭī.

Core Idea
Sanusi's writings are praised for their flawless expression and universal acceptance.

This lesson presents two scholarly testimonies that highlight the excellence of Imam al-Sanusi's authored works. Al-Shafshāwī praises the illumination and perfection of his expression, while al-Hibṭī asserts that his speech is free from errors. These statements underscore the authority and reliability of Sanusi's texts in the eyes of his contemporaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: two separate testimonies from different scholars.
The passage includes footnotes that are part of the original text.
2
Identify the scholars and key phrases
Find the names of the two scholars: al-Shafshāwī and al-Hibṭī. Note the phrases they use: 'tanwīr kalamihi' (illumination of his speech) and 'maḥfūẓ min al-saqaṭāt' (preserved from slips).
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why such praise is important for studying Sanusi's works. The scholars are not just praising his piety but the quality and accuracy of his writings.
This connects to the idea of 'sanad' (chain of authority) in Islamic scholarship.

And al-Shafshāwī described the authorship of Imam al-Sanusi with a comprehensive statement, saying: 'How excellent is the illumination of his speech, the perfection of his expression, so much so that the obstinate person finds no entry for objection in any way or manner, and the agreement of the elite of the saints and the great scholars on his virtue and the acceptance of his works.' Commentary
This passage is a collection of two scholarly testimonies praising Imam al-Sanusi's writings. First, al-Shafshāwī gives a comprehensive description: Sanusi's words are luminous and his expression so perfect that a critic can find no way to object; moreover, the great saints and scholars agree on his virtue and accept his works. Second, al-Hibṭī states that Sanusi's speech is 'preserved from slips' (maḥfūẓ min al-saqaṭāt), meaning it is free from errors or lapses. These two quotes highlight the high esteem in which Sanusi's scholarly output was held.

Sanusi's writings were held in the highest regard by leading scholars for their clarity, perfection, and freedom from error.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's writings were so excellent that even critics could find no flaw.

Imam al-Sanusi was not only a theologian but also a prolific author in various fields. His contemporaries recognized the high quality of his works. In this passage, we read two scholars' testimonies about the excellence of his writings, emphasizing their clarity, perfection, and freedom from error. This helps us appreciate the authority his works carry.

This passage presents biographical information about Imam al-Sanusi's writings and the praise they received from two contemporary scholars. First, it mentions two of his medical works: a treatise on medicine and a commentary on Ibn Sina's poem. It then warns against spurious attributions, stating that other titles claimed to be his are definitely not authentic, so there is no need to address them. The core of the passage is al-Shifshāwunī's comprehensive praise: he highlights the clarity (tanwīr) and perfection (itqān) of al-Sanusi's expression, to the point that no critic can find any flaw (ta‘aqqub) in them. The passage concludes with footnotes referencing the source 'Dawhat al-Nashir' for these quotes.

The Noble Character of Imam al-Sanusi
Narrates al-Sanusi's exemplary moral qualities and spiritual state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive moral and spiritual qualities of Imam al-Sanusi as described by his biographer.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's luminous nature combined vast knowledge with profound humility and mercy.

This passage offers a vivid portrait of Imam al-Sanusi's character, painting him as a person whose inner virtues matched his scholarly stature. The author describes him as a model of piety, compassion, and humility, emphasizing that his entire conduct was surrounded by divine care. Reading this, we see how the traditional ideal of a scholar combines both knowledge and character.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic description
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, noticing the flow of attributes. Then read the English translation.
The passage uses a series of adjectives to describe his character.
2
Identify the key qualities
List the virtues mentioned: piety, asceticism, patience, mercy, humility, shyness, etc. Notice how each is described.
Some qualities are internal (mercy, fear) while others are external (few words, charity).
3
Understand the summary statement
Reflect on the concluding phrase: 'his clay was luminous and his conduct surrounded by divine care.' This summarizes his entire being.
The phrase 'ṭīna nūrāniyya' suggests a pure, light-filled nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبس من عظيم أخلاقه جمع الله تعالى للإمام السنوسي إلى عظيم علمه وبحبوحة معرفتِه .. سعةَ الأخلاق الحميدة ، فجابَ شُعب الإيمان وطنَّبَ فيها خيامه ، فعُهدَ عنه أنه خيرٌ بر ، تقي ورع زاهد ، حلیم صبور ، سكنتِ الرحمة فؤاده، وغلبت عليه الشفقة على جميع خلق الله تعالى ، هيّنُ لينُ القياد ، شديد الحياء ، كثير التواضع ، قليل الكلام والضحك ، تجلَّى عليه الخوف من الله تعالى ؛ فأكثر من إحياء الليل مناجياً ، وأكثر من الصدقة والدعاء راجياً وبالجملة : كانت طينته نورانية ، وسيرته محفوفة بعناية ربانية
A spark from his great character. Allah Almighty combined for Imam al-Sanusi, along with his vast knowledge and abundance of his cognition, a breadth of praiseworthy character. So he traversed the branches of faith and pitched his tents in them. It was known of him that he was good, pious, righteous, ascetic, forbearing, patient. Mercy settled in his heart, and compassion for all creatures of Allah Almighty overwhelmed him. He was gentle, easy to lead, extremely shy, very humble, spoke little and laughed little. The fear of Allah Almighty appeared upon him, so he revived the night in intimate prayer, and increased in charity and supplication, hoping. In summary: his clay was luminous, and his conduct was surrounded by a divine care.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage describes the noble character (akhlāq ḥamīda) of Imam al-Sanusi, presenting it as a complement to his immense knowledge. The author states that God combined for al-Sanusi vast knowledge (ʿilm), abundant cognition (maʿrifa), and a breadth of praiseworthy character. This character is then detailed in a series of attributes: he was good (khayr), pious (birr), righteous (taqī), ascetic (zāhid), forbearing (ḥalīm), patient (ṣabūr). Mercy filled his heart and compassion for all creatures dominated him. He was gentle (hayyin), easy to lead (layyin al-qiyād), extremely shy (shadīd al-ḥayā’), very humble (kathīr al-tawāḍuʿ), spoke and laughed little (qalīl al-kalām wa al-ḍaḥik). The fear of God was manifest on him, leading him to revive the night with intimate prayer (munājāt) and to increase in charity and supplication. The passage concludes with a summary: his primordial nature (ṭīna) was luminous (nūrāniyya), and his entire conduct (sīra) was enveloped by divine care (ʿināya rabbāniyya). The description emphasizes both internal virtues (mercy, fear, humility) and external practices (prayer, charity).

Key Terms
طينة نورانية
شعب الإيمان
الأخلاق الحميدة
الشفقة
حياء
تواضع
مناجاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'kathīr al-tawāḍuʿ' (very humble) might be misunderstood as lacking self-esteem or being weak.
Correction: In Islamic tradition, tawāḍuʿ is a praiseworthy humility before God and people, not self-deprecation. It reflects strength of character, not weakness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a trait of al-Sanusi's character?
A) Patience (ṣabūr)
B) Asceticism (zuhd)
C) Eloquence in speech
D) Compassion for all creatures

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi embodied the ideal of a scholar whose vast knowledge was matched by luminous character and deep compassion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Sanusi's mercy settled in his heart and compassion overwhelmed him. What occupies the depths of your heart when you are alone? If compassion is not there, what is, and how can you begin to invite mercy in?
?
The fear of God drove him to revive the night in prayer and charity. When you lie in bed at night, what drives you—fear of missing worldly pleasures or fear of standing before your Lord? What will you change tonight to align your sleep and your soul?
?
He was described as extremely shy and very humble. When was the last time you felt true shyness before Allah—enough to stop you from a sin or to silence your ego? Is your character marked by such humility, or by pride that you justify? What would it take for you to become like him?

Testimonies to al-Sanusi's Vast Knowledge
Quotes scholars affirming al-Sanusi's scholarly breadth and depth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi's contemporaries testified to his scholarly breadth and spiritual depth.

Core Idea
Testimonies from great scholars confirm al-Sanusi's unmatched knowledge and renewal.

This passage presents the testimonies of two prominent scholars—al-Shifshāwunī and al-Ḥaḍīkī—who personally witnessed al-Sanusi's scholarly output and spiritual state. Their words provide external validation of his status as a renewer and a master of both exoteric and esoteric sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Let the words of the scholars speak directly before any analysis.
Notice the structure: a general claim followed by two specific quotes.
2
Identify the two testimonies
First testimony from al-Shifshāwunī highlights al-Sanusi's role as a renewer (mujaddid) and his scholarly verification (taḥqīq). Second testimony from al-Ḥaḍīkī emphasizes his mastery of both exoteric and esoteric sciences and his intense God-consciousness (murāqaba).
Each testimony focuses on different aspects of his knowledge.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why are these testimonies important? They place al-Sanusi among the greatest scholars and saints, affirming that his work in ʿaqīda is built on deep spiritual and intellectual foundations.
Such endorsements from contemporaries lend authority to his theological works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما سعة علمه : فآثاره العلمية الواسعة تشهد له بذلك ، ولا سيما علم التوحيد والتصوف قال فيه العلامة الشفشاوني : ( كان من مشايخ المئة التاسعة ، وتوفي على رأسها ، فكان من جدَّد لهذه الأمة أمر دينها على رأس تلك المئة كما أخبر الصادق المصدوق صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان من أكابر الأولياء ، وأعلام العلماء ، وتآليفه تدلُّ على تحقيقه وغزارة علمه )(۱) وقال العلامة الحضيكي : ( كان آية في العلم والصلاح والهدى والزهد والورع ، له أوفر حظ في العلوم فروعها وأصولها ، إذا تحدث في علم ظنَّ السامع أنه لا يحسن غيره ، لا سيما في التوحيد ، وانفرد بعلم الباطن ، لا يقرأ شيئاً من علم الظاهر إلا خرج لعلوم الآخرة لا سيما في التفسير والحديث ، كأنه يشاهد الآخرة ؛ لكثرة مراقبته الله تعالى ((۲) (۱) انظر ( دوحة الناشر » ( ص ۱۲۱ ) . (۲) انظر (طبقات الحضيكي » ( ص ٢٣٥ ) .
As for the breadth of his knowledge: his extensive scientific works testify to that, especially the science of tawḥīd (monotheism) and taṣawwuf (Sufism). The scholar al-Shifshāwunī said of him: 'He was among the shaykhs of the ninth century, and he died at its beginning. He was among those who renewed for this nation the matter of its religion at the beginning of that century, as the truthful and believed (peace be upon him) informed. He was among the greatest of saints and the foremost scholars, and his works indicate his verification (taḥqīq) and the abundance of his knowledge.' (1) And the scholar al-Ḥaḍīkī said: 'He was a sign (āya) in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety. He had the fullest share of the sciences, both branches and roots. When he spoke about a science, the listener would think he knew nothing else, especially in tawḥīd. He was unique in the esoteric science (ʿilm al-bāṭin). He would not read anything from the exoteric sciences (ʿilm al-ẓāhir) except that it led him to the sciences of the afterlife, especially in tafsīr and ḥadīth. He would see the afterlife as if he were witnessing it, due to his abundant observance (murāqaba) of Allah Almighty.' (2) (1) See 'Dawhat al-Nāshir' (p. 121). (2) See 'Ṭabaqāt al-Ḥaḍīkī' (p. 235).
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage focuses on the breadth of al-Sanusi's knowledge (saʿat ʿilmihi), arguing that his extensive scientific works (āthār ʿilmiyya) are sufficient testimony, especially in the fields of tawḥīd and taṣawwuf. Two prominent scholars are quoted. First, al-Shifshāwunī places al-Sanusi in the ninth century hijri and identifies him as a renewer (mujaddid) of religion for this nation, as foretold in a hadith. He calls him among the greatest saints (akābir al-awliyā’) and foremost scholars (aʿlām al-ʿulamā’), and states that his works demonstrate his verification (taḥqīq) and abundant knowledge. Second, al-Ḥaḍīkī emphasizes that al-Sanusi was a 'sign' (āya) in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety. He had the fullest share of the sciences, both branches (furūʿ) and roots (uṣūl). His mastery was such that listeners would think he only knew the subject he was discussing, especially in tawḥīd. He was unique in the esoteric science (ʿilm al-bāṭin), and his engagement with exoteric sciences (such as tafsīr and ḥadīth) always led him to the sciences of the afterlife. His intense observance (murāqaba) of God made him almost see the afterlife. The passage concludes with footnotes referencing the sources.

Key Terms

التصوف
التجديد
أعلام العلماء
تحقيقه
علم الباطن
مراقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'lā yaqraʾu shayʾan min ʿilm al-ẓāhir illā kharaja liʿulūm al-ākhira' is sometimes misinterpreted to mean that al-Sanusi rejected or disregarded exoteric learning.
Correction: In fact, it means he always derived spiritual wisdom from his studies of exoteric subjects like tafsīr and ḥadīth, showing his deep integration of both domains.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ḥaḍīkī, what was al-Sanusi's unique distinction?
A) He wrote many books
B) He was a renewer of the century
C) He combined mastery of both exoteric and esoteric sciences
D) He taught all sciences equally

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's vast knowledge was recognized by his peers as spanning both exoteric and esoteric sciences, making him a renewer of the century.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have any witness in your own life—someone who can testify that your knowledge leads you closer to Allah? Who would that be, and what would they say?
?
When you study any Islamic science—fiqh, tafsīr, or even your daily reading—does it always drive your heart toward the akhira, or are you content with just information?
?
Al-Sanusi was called a 'sign' (āya) in knowledge and piety. What one area of your life—knowledge, worship, or character—would you want to become a sign for others to see Allah? What will you change today to move toward that?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi

Sanusi's Vast Knowledge and the True Scholar's Signs
Biographical account of Sanusi's knowledge with definition of a scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of Sanusi's knowledge as described by his contemporaries and his own criteria for a true scholar.

Core Idea
Sanusi combined inner and outer sciences, defining a true scholar by deep understanding.

This passage from the commentary on Sanusi's works presents a biographical sketch highlighting his extraordinary scholarship. It includes testimonies from two prominent scholars and Sanusi's own definition of a true scholar, emphasizing both breadth and depth of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall tribute. Notice the structure: opening summary, then testimonies, then Sanusi's own definition, then a final quote.
2
Identify the two testimonies
Find the quotes from al-Shifshāwnī and al-Ḥaḍīkī. Note what each emphasizes about Sanusi: his role as a renewer, his mastery of both outward and inward sciences, especially tawḥīd.
3
Analyze Sanusi's definition of a scholar
Read Sanusi's own words: 'The true scholar is one who finds difficult what is clear and clarifies what is difficult...' Reflect on what this means for scholarly character.
This definition emphasizes depth and verification, not merely accumulation of facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : كانت طينته نورانية ، وسيرته محفوفة بعناية ربانية
أما سعة علمه :
فآثاره العلمية الواسعة تشهد له بذلك ، ولا سيما علم التوحيد والتصوف
قال فيه العلامة الشفشاوني : ( كان من مشايخ المئة التاسعة ، وتوفي على رأسها ، فكان من جدَّد لهذه الأمة أمر دينها على رأس تلك المئة كما أخبر الصادق المصدوق صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان من أكابر الأولياء ، وأعلام العلماء ، وتآليفه تدلُّ على تحقيقه وغزارة

وقال العلامة الحضيكي : ( كان آية في العلم والصلاح والهدى والزهد والورع ، له أوفر حظ في العلوم فروعها وأصولها ، إذا تحدث في علم ظنَّ السامع أنه لا يحسن غيره ، لا سيما في التوحيد ، وانفرد بعلم الباطن ، لا يقرأ شيئاً من علم الظاهر إلا خرج لعلوم الآخرة لا سيما في التفسير والحديث ، كأنه يشاهد الآخرة ؛ لكثرة مراقبته الله تعالى ((۲)
وقد جعل رحمه الله تعالى للعالم علامات يعرف بها ؛ حيث قال :
( العالم حقاً : من يستشكل الواضح ، ويوضح المشكل ؛ لسعة فهمه وعلمه وتحقيقه ، فهو الذي يُحضر مجلسه ، ويُستمع فوائده )(۱) وقال العلامة ابن مريم في البستان ) : ( أما علومه الظاهرة فله
فيها أوفر نصيب ، والتعصيب ) (٢)
And in summary: his clay was luminous, and his conduct was surrounded by divine care. As for the vastness of his knowledge: his extensive scholarly works testify to that, especially the science of tawḥīd (monotheism) and taṣawwuf (Sufism). Al-Shifshāwnī said about him: 'He was among the shaykhs of the ninth century and died at its beginning, and he was one of those who renewed for this community the matters of its religion at the head of that century, as the truthful and verified one (PBUH) informed, and he was among the greatest awliyā' (saints) and prominent scholars, and his writings indicate his verification and abundance of knowledge.' Al-Ḥaḍīkī said: 'He was a sign in knowledge, righteousness, guidance, asceticism, and piety; he had the greatest share in the sciences, both their branches and their roots; when he spoke on a science, the listener would think that he knew nothing else, especially in tawḥīd, and he was unique in the science of the inner (bāṭin); he would not read anything of the outward science except that it came out for the sciences of the Hereafter, especially in tafsīr and ḥadīth, as if he were witnessing the Hereafter due to his abundant mindfulness of Allah.' And he (may Allah have mercy on him) set signs by which the scholar is known, where he said: 'The true scholar is one who finds difficult what is clear and clarifies what is difficult, due to the breadth of his understanding, knowledge, and verification; so he is the one whose gathering should be attended and whose benefits should be listened to.' And al-ʿAllāma Ibn Maryam said in al-Bustān: 'As for his outward sciences, he has the greatest share in them, and authorization.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account praising Imam Sanusi's knowledge. It begins with a summarizing statement that his nature was luminous and his conduct divinely guided. Then it turns to the topic of his vast knowledge, stating that his extensive scholarly works testify to it, especially in tawḥīd and taṣawwuf. The author then provides two external scholarly testimonies. First, al-Shifshāwnī describes him as a renewer of the religion at the turn of the ninth century, a great saint and scholar, whose writings show his depth. Second, al-Ḥaḍīkī emphasizes his excellence in both the branches and roots of knowledge, his specialism in tawḥīd, his unique mastery of the inner sciences (bāṭin), and his mindfulness of Allah to the point of seeming to witness the Hereafter. After these quotes, the passage presents Sanusi's own definition of a true scholar: one who finds clear things difficult (i.e., does not rush to judgment) and clarifies difficult matters, due to his deep understanding and verification. Such a person is worthy of being attended and listened to. Finally, Ibn Maryam briefly confirms his great share in outward sciences and his authorization. The passage thus builds a comprehensive picture of Sanusi's scholarly stature through external praise, his own words, and further attestation.

Correction: The passage shows he was equally proficient in outward sciences like tafsir and hadith, and his works in tawḥīd and taṣawwuf are highly regarded.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Ḥaḍīkī described Sanusi as reading outward sciences only to extract lessons for the afterlife. Reflect on your last study session—was your intention solely to know, or to draw closer to Allah? If your heart feels distant, what specific change in your study habits can you make today to realign your intention?
?
Sanusi combined mastery of both inner and outer sciences. Which area are you neglecting—your outward actions or your inward state? Pick one concrete practice from the outward (e.g., a sunnah you neglect) and one from the inner (e.g., a bad thought you indulge) and decide right now how you will work on both this week.

Sanusi's Definition of the True Wali
Sanusi's definition of the true wali and commentary on asceticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of the true wali as one who prioritizes his Lord over even Paradise, and how this exemplifies extreme asceticism.

Core Idea
The true wali turns from Paradise itself to focus solely on Allah.

Imam Sanusi was renowned not only for his theological acumen but also for his profound spirituality. In this passage, he gives a striking definition of the true wali that reveals the depth of his own detachment from worldly desires, including the desire for Paradise. This saying has been widely transmitted in his biographies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Focus on Sanusi's own words. Notice the structure: he presents a definition and then a comment.
The ellipsis in the Arabic indicates a pause; do not worry about it.
2
Analyze the meaning of 'not turning to Paradise over his Lord'
This does not mean he dislikes Paradise, but that his focus on Allah is so complete that even the highest reward does not distract him.
This is the core of Sanusi's understanding of true sainthood.
3
Connect to the narrator's comment about asceticism
The narrator says that one who is ascetic regarding such lofty things is even more ascetic regarding lesser worldly things. This shows Sanusi's extreme detachment.
The comment underscores that Sanusi's own asceticism is rooted in this perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وجمي من فروعها وأصولها السهم
فحسبك بكلمته التي تنوقلت في ترجمته ؛ حيث قال : ( حقيقة
الولي العارف : من لو كشف له الجنة وحورها . . ما التفت إليها عن ولا ركن لغيره تعالى ((۳) ، فمن زهد بمثل هذا فهو عما دونه أزهد .

And as for his asceticism and piety: His share from its branches and roots is abundant. It suffices you with his saying that has been transmitted in his biography, where he said: 'The reality of the knowing wali (al-walī al-ʿārif) is: if Paradise and its houris were unveiled to him, he would not turn to them over [turning to] his Lord, nor would he rely on anything other than Him.' And the sultan sent to him to take some of the revenues of the school of the righteous wali al-Ḥasan Abarkān, but he refused. Commentary
This passage describes Imam Sanusi's asceticism and piety. It begins with a line emphasizing his abundant share in these qualities. Then it presents his own famous saying about the true wali: even if Paradise with its houris were shown to him, he would not turn away from his Lord nor rely on anything else. The narrator comments that one who is ascetic regarding such tremendous rewards is even more ascetic regarding lesser things. Next, an anecdote illustrates his scrupulousness: the sultan asked him to take some revenues from a school, but Sanusi refused, insisting until he wrote a lengthy apology which was accepted. Finally, the passage notes his general character: he was not familiar with anyone, did not seek fame, and disliked the company of worldly people, looking at them, or being near them. The fragment at the end suggests he was harsh toward those who are opulent in this world. The passage thus shows his extreme detachment from worldly possessions and status.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that true asceticism means rejecting even the desire for Paradise.
Correction: Sanusi's point is that the true wali does not turn away from Paradise out of dislike, but because his attachment to Allah is paramount; he would still enter Paradise but his focus is beyond it.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's definition of the true wali sets the highest standard: no created reward, even Paradise, should distract from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the veil were lifted and Paradise stood before you, would your heart remain exclusively fixed on Allah, or would it be drawn to the reward? What does your current prayer—when you are alone—reveal about your ultimate desire? Commit this week to making at least one sujood where you consciously focus only on Allah, not on what He gives.
?
Sanusi's asceticism was so complete that he would not turn to Paradise. What 'lesser matters'—even permissible pleasures—still pull your heart away from Allah? Name one such attachment and set a specific time today to detach from it, even if only for a few minutes, as an act of love for Allah alone.
?
The commentator says that one who is ascetic regarding Paradise is even more ascetic regarding lesser things. Reflect: If you find yourself indifferent to Paradise for the sake of Allah, does that indifference extend to your worldly worries? What does your anxiety about daily affairs say about your level of asceticism? Today, recite the hadith 'Allah is enough for me' (حسبي الله) 100 times with presence of heart to train your reliance on Him alone.

Sanusi's Encounter with the Sultan and His Character
Anecdote about Sanusi's refusal of sultan's request and his character traits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's scrupulousness (wara') in refusing the sultan's request for school revenues, and his general disposition to avoid worldly company.

Core Idea
Sanusi's piety meant refusing even legitimate worldly gains from a sultan.

Asceticism is not just about thoughts but actions. The passage illustrates Sanusi's practical scrupulousness when the sultan asked him to take some revenues from a school. His refusal and the subsequent anecdote show his care to keep his hands clean of any possible misuse. Additionally, a description of his character reveals his deliberate avoidance of worldly associations.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote about the sultan's request
Pay attention to the details: the sultan sent to take revenues from the school of al-Ḥasan Abarkān, and Sanusi refused.
This shows his scrupulousness even with a direct request from the ruler.
2
Understand Sanusi's response: refusal, insistence, and written excuse
He did not simply refuse; he wrote a lengthy excuse that was accepted. This indicates his carefulness and authority.
The acceptance of his excuse shows that his scrupulousness was respected.
3
Read the description of his character regarding worldly people
He was not intimate with anyone, did not seek fame, and disliked the company of worldly people.
This reinforces his detachment and focus on the spiritual.

It suffices you with his saying that has been transmitted in his biography, where he said: 'The reality of the knowing wali (al-walī al-ʿārif) is: if Paradise and its houris were unveiled to him, he would not turn to them over [turning to] his Lord, nor would he rely on anything other than Him.' And the sultan sent to him to take some of the revenues of the school of the righteous wali al-Ḥasan Abarkān, but he refused. They insisted on him, so he wrote a lengthy excuse in writing, and it was accepted from him. He was not intimate with anyone and did not seek to be known, and he disliked associating with the people of this world, looking at them, and being near them. And he—for those who are expansive for them in it...
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage describes Imam Sanusi's asceticism and piety. It begins with a line emphasizing his abundant share in these qualities. Then it presents his own famous saying about the true wali: even if Paradise with its houris were shown to him, he would not turn away from his Lord nor rely on anything else. The narrator comments that one who is ascetic regarding such tremendous rewards is even more ascetic regarding lesser things. Next, an anecdote illustrates his scrupulousness: the sultan asked him to take some revenues from a school, but Sanusi refused, insisting until he wrote a lengthy apology which was accepted. Finally, the passage notes his general character: he was not familiar with anyone, did not seek fame, and disliked the company of worldly people, looking at them, or being near them. The fragment at the end suggests he was harsh toward those who are opulent in this world. The passage thus shows his extreme detachment from worldly possessions and status.

Key Terms
السلطان
الغلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that scrupulousness means refusing all interactions with authority.
Correction: Sanusi refused a specific request for revenues, but he engaged with the sultan through written correspondence, showing that scrupulousness is about lawful propriety, not rejection of authority.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi refuse to take revenues from the school?
A) He considered the school illegitimate
B) He was scrupulous and wrote a lengthy excuse
C) He disliked the sultan personally
D) He wanted the revenues for himself

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's wara' (scrupulousness) led him to avoid even potentially lawful gains to maintain spiritual purity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi refused a legitimate offer from the sultan. Is there any worldly gain you are currently chasing that, while permissible, might compromise your spiritual focus? What would it cost you to let it go?
?
Sanusi disliked associating with people of this world. Who are the people in your life that draw your attention away from Allah? Take a moment to honestly assess: do you seek their company more than the company of the righteous?
?
Sanusi did not seek to be known. Have you ever secretly desired recognition for your piety or knowledge? How can you actively hide your good deeds this week to purify your intention?

The True Scholar and the Reality of Zuhd
Definition of true scholar and zuhd principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining characteristics of a true scholar and the essence of zuhd as exemplified by the scholar's statement about the saintly knower.

Core Idea
A true scholar's asceticism makes him turn from even Paradise for God.

This passage opens by describing the true scholar as one who questions the clear and clarifies the ambiguous. Then it presents a profound statement on zuhd: the saintly knower would not turn from God even if Paradise were unveiled. This sets the standard for the scholar's detachment from worldly concerns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the definition and the key statement on zuhd.
Notice the author uses a quote from Ibn Maryam to introduce the scholar's zuhd.
2
Identify the two key definitions
Find the definition of a true scholar and the definition of a saintly knower. Notice how they complement each other.
The true scholar is intellectually sharp; the saintly knower is spiritually detached.
3
Reflect on the meaning of zuhd
Consider why turning away from Paradise for God is the highest form of asceticism. What does this reveal about the scholar's spiritual state?
Zuhd here is not just leaving worldly things; it's a total focus on the Creator.

( العالم حقاً : من يستشكل الواضح ، ويوضح المشكل ؛ لسعة فهمه وعلمه وتحقيقه ، فهو الذي يُحضر مجلسه ، ويُستمع فوائده ) وقال العلامة ابن مريم في البستان ) : ( أما علومه الظاهرة فله فيها أوفر نصيب ، والتعصيب ) وأما زهده وورعه : فحسبك بكلمته التي تنوقلت في ترجمته ؛ حيث قال : ( حقيقة الولي العارف : من لو كشف له الجنة وحورها . . ما التفت إليها عن ولا ركن لغيره تعالى ) ، فمن زهد بمثل هذا فهو عما دونه أزهد . وقد بعث إليه السلطان في أخذ شيء من غلات مدرسة الولي الصالح الحسن أبركان ، فامتنع ، فألحوا عليه ، فكتب في الاعتذار كتابة مطوّلة ، فقُبل منه كان لا يأنس بأحدٍ ولا يتسبب في معرفته ، وقد تبغض إليه الاجتماع بأهل الدنيا والنظر إليهم وقربهم ، وهو لمن بسط لهم فيها ونالوا رتبها .. أشدُّ بغضاً ، قال تلميذه الملالي : ( خرج يوماً معنا للصحراء ، فرأى فرساناً بثياب فاخرة على بُعد ، فقال : من هؤلاء ؟ قلنا : خواص السلطان ، فتعوذ ورجع لطريق آخر ولقيهم مرة أخرى ، وما تمكن من الرجوع ، فجعل وجهه للحائط وغطاه حتى جازوا ولم يروه ) . ولما أراد ختم التفسير عزم على قراءة سورة ( الإخلاص ) يوماً و ( المعوذتين ) يوماً ، فسمع به الوزير وأراد حضور الختم ، فبلغه فقرأ السور الثلاثة يوماً واحداً ؛ خيفة حضور الوزير عنده ذلك ، ورؤيته والاجتماع به وطلبه السلطان أن يطلع إليه ويقرأ التفسير بحضرته على عادة المفسرين ، فامتنع ، فألحوا عليه ، فكتب إليه معتذراً بغلبة الحياء له ولا يقدر على التكلم هناك ، فأيسوا منه وإذا سمع يوماً بوليمة أحد أبناء الدنيا تخلف من الحضور ؛ خيفة أن يُدعى ، فلا يظهرُ بالكلية حتى تمر أيام الوليمة وربما تخلف قبله أياماً وحاشى نفسه عن قبول عطايا السلطان ومن لاذ به ، وربما تأتي داره وهو غائب ، فإذا وجدها أنكر على أهل داره ، وتغيّر كثيراً ، وكان
(The true scholar: one who questions the clear and clarifies the ambiguous, due to the breadth of his understanding, knowledge, and verification; so he is the one whose gathering is attended and whose benefits are heard.) And the scholar Ibn Maryam said in 'al-Bustan': (As for his apparent sciences, he has the greatest share in them, and the boosting.) As for his asceticism and piety: sufficient for you is his statement that was transmitted in his biography: (The reality of the saintly knower: one who, if the Garden and its maidens were unveiled to him, would not turn to them from [the truth] nor rely on other than God the Exalted.) So whoever is ascetic regarding such a thing is even more ascetic regarding what is below it. The sultan sent to him to take some of the produce of the school of the righteous saint Hasan Abarkan, and he refused. They pressed him, so he wrote a lengthy apology, and it was accepted from him. He did not feel at ease with anyone nor did he seek to be known, and he detested association with people of this world, looking at them, and proximity to them. He was most hateful toward those who were given [worldly things] and attained ranks. His student al-Mallali said: 'One day he went out with us to the desert. He saw riders in fine clothes in the distance and said, 'Who are these?' We said, 'The sultan's special retinue.' So he sought refuge and returned by another road.' He met them another time and could not turn back, so he put his face to the wall and covered it until they passed and did not see him. When he wanted to complete the commentary [on the Qur'an], he resolved to recite Surah al-Ikhlas on one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn on another day. The vizier heard of this and wanted to attend the completion. This reached him, so he recited the three surahs in one day, fearing the vizier's presence with him, seeing him, and meeting him. The sultan requested that he come to him and read the commentary in his presence, as was the custom of commentators. He refused. They pressed him, so he wrote to him apologizing, [saying] that shyness overcomes him and he cannot speak there. So they despaired of him. If he heard one day of a banquet of one of the sons of this world, he would stay away from attending, fearing that he might be called, and he would not appear at all until the days of the banquet passed, and perhaps he would stay away days before it. He kept himself above accepting gifts from the sultan and those who took refuge with him. Sometimes gifts would come to his house while he was absent. If he found them, he would rebuke his family and become very upset, and he was...

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of a scholar's asceticism (zuhd) and piety (wara'). It begins with a general definition of a true scholar as one who questions what is clear and clarifies what is ambiguous, then mentions the scholar's extensive knowledge. The focus then shifts to his asceticism, illustrated by his famous statement: a true saintly knower would not turn from God even if Paradise were unveiled to him. The passage then narrates several specific incidents demonstrating his avoidance of worldly authority and wealth: he refused the sultan's request for school produce, he avoided the sultan's retinue by turning away or covering his face, he changed his recitation schedule to avoid the vizier's presence, he refused the sultan's invitation to read commentary, he avoided banquets of worldly people, and he rejected gifts from the sultan. Each anecdote reinforces his extreme caution to avoid any association with worldly power and status, even when such association might seem harmless or even beneficial. The passage concludes by noting that he would rebuke his family if they accepted gifts in his absence.

Key Terms
زهد (zuhd)
ورع (wara')
ولي (wali)
عارف ('arif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zuhd means rejecting all material possessions and social interaction.
Correction: Zuhd, as shown here, is not about rejecting all physical things but about spiritual detachment—not being distracted from God even by the highest reward like Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of the saintly knower?
A) One who has extensive knowledge of apparent sciences.
B) One who, even if Paradise and its maidens were unveiled to him, would not turn from God.
C) One who avoids all social gatherings.
D) One who questions the clear and clarifies the ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The highest zuhd is to be so devoted to God that even Paradise is secondary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The true scholar questions even what seems clear. What basic belief about God or your life have you never truly examined? Will you take time today to search it deeply?
?
If Paradise itself were offered to you but it meant turning away from God, would you be able to refuse? What does that reveal about where your heart truly lies?
?
Who or what do you rely on besides God—your health, your job, your relationships? Are you ready to let go of that crutch and trust only Him, even if it costs you everything?

Avoiding Worldly Authority and Its People
Anecdotes demonstrating avoidance of rulers and worldly people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe several practical examples of how the scholar avoided worldly authority and understand the underlying principle of wara'.

Core Idea
True piety avoids even the appearance of attachment to rulers and worldly people.

After establishing the principle of zuhd, the biography offers vivid anecdotes showing how the scholar lived this principle. He refused the sultan's requests, avoided his retinue, changed his plans to evade the vizier, and rejected gifts. These stories illustrate his extreme caution in preserving his spiritual focus.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdotes sequentially
Read each incident in the Arabic and translation: refusal of produce, avoiding riders, reciting surahs together, refusal of reading in sultan's presence, avoiding banquets, rejecting gifts.
Notice the pattern of precaution and avoidance.
2
Identify the common pattern
Ask: What does each story share? The scholar goes out of his way to avoid contact with rulers and worldly authorities, even changing plans or covering his face.
His fear is not of the people themselves but of spiritual harm from association.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why the scholar took such extreme measures. What does this tell us about wara' (piety) and protection of one's faith?
Wara' often involves avoiding even permissible things that might lead to something harmful.

?

?

?

The Scholar's Avoidance of Rulers
Three anecdotes about a scholar's avoidance of rulers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the religious motivations behind the scholar's deliberate avoidance of rulers and worldly gatherings.

Core Idea
True piety often means avoiding the company of the powerful.

This lesson presents three vivid anecdotes from the life of a great scholar, illustrating his consistent effort to stay away from rulers and their entourage. These stories are not merely biographical curiosities; they reflect a deep spiritual principle: that proximity to worldly power can endanger one's faith. As you read, notice the lengths he goes to avoid even being seen by them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the repeated theme of avoidance.
The scholar's student is narrating, giving an eyewitness account.
2
Identify the three anecdotes
The passage contains three distinct incidents. Try to identify each one and what the scholar did to avoid the ruler's people.
The first is about horsemen, the second about the vizier, the third about the sultan.
3
Reflect on the motivations
Consider why the scholar went to such extremes. The passage mentions 'fear' and 'modesty' as reasons. What does this teach us about piety?
This connects to the common misunderstanding that he was anti-social.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال تلميذه الملالي : ( خرج يوماً معنا للصحراء ، فرأى فرساناً بثياب فاخرة على بُعد ، فقال : من هؤلاء ؟ قلنا : خواص السلطان ، فتعوذ ورجع لطريق آخر . ولقيهم مرة أخرى ، وما تمكن من الرجوع ، فجعل وجهه للحائط وغطاه حتى جازوا ولم يروه . ولما أراد ختم التفسير عزم على قراءة سورة ( الإخلاص ) يوماً و ( المعوذتين ) يوماً ، فسمع به الوزير وأراد حضور الختم ، فبلغه فقرأ السور الثلاثة يوماً واحداً ؛ خيفة حضور الوزير عنده ذلك ، ورؤيته والاجتماع به . وطلبه السلطان أن يطلع إليه ويقرأ التفسير بحضرته على عادة المفسرين ، فامتنع ، فألحوا عليه ، فكتب إليه معتذراً بغلبة الحياء له ولا يقدر على التكلم هناك ، فأيسوا منه .
His student al-Mallali said: (One day he went out with us to the desert, and he saw horsemen in fine clothes in the distance, and said: Who are these? We said: The special retinue of the sultan. So he sought refuge and returned by another road. And he met them another time, and was not able to turn back, so he put his face to the wall and covered it until they passed and did not see him. And when he wanted to complete the commentary (tafsir), he resolved to read Surah al-Ikhlas one day and al-Mu'awwidhatayn another day, but the vizier heard about it and wanted to attend the completion ceremony. When that reached him, he read the three surahs in one day, out of fear of the vizier attending him on that occasion and of seeing him and meeting him. And the sultan requested that he come to him and read the commentary in his presence, as was the custom of the commentators, but he refused. They pressed him, so he wrote to him apologizing due to overwhelming modesty, saying he was not able to speak there, so they despaired of him.)
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents biographical anecdotes about the scholar's extreme avoidance of rulers and their entourage. The first anecdote describes how he changed his path upon seeing the sultan's horsemen, and when unable to avoid them another time, he turned his face to the wall so as not to see them or be seen. The second anecdote relates to his completion of a tafsir work: he planned to recite Surah al-Ikhlas on one day and the Mu'awwidhatayn on another, but upon learning that the vizier intended to attend the ceremony, he recited all three in one day to avoid the vizier's presence. The third anecdote tells of the sultan's request for him to present his tafsir in person; he refused firmly, even after being pressured, citing his overwhelming modesty as an excuse. The common thread is the scholar's deliberate and consistent effort to avoid worldly authority, which he sees as a source of spiritual harm.

Key Terms
الخيفة
الحياء
السلطان
التفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The scholar was anti-social or disliked people.
Correction: His avoidance was motivated by religious piety and fear of spiritual harm, not misanthropy.

Checkpoint
Why did the scholar read all three surahs in one day instead of spreading them over two days?
A) He was in a hurry to complete the tafsir.
B) He feared that the vizier would attend the completion ceremony and wanted to avoid him.
C) He forgot his original plan.
D) He wanted to show off his memorization.

Key Takeaway
The scholar's extreme measures to avoid rulers demonstrate that preserving one's spiritual state can require sacrificing worldly honor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you, like this scholar, chosen to inconvenience yourself or sacrifice a worldly opportunity to protect your spiritual state? If you cannot recall such a moment, why is that?
?
Consider a time when you sought the approval of someone in authority or a person of status. How did that affect your relationship with Allah? Are you willing to change that today?
?
The scholar's 'overwhelming modesty' kept him from speaking in the sultan's presence. What is your 'overwhelming modesty'—or lack of it—before Allah? Are you more afraid of human opinion than Divine pleasure?

Rejecting Gifts from Rulers
Anecdote about rejecting gifts from rulers and accepting only from lawful earners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scholar refused gifts from rulers and those associated with them, and how he accepted gifts from those with lawful earnings.

Core Idea
He rejected gifts from rulers to preserve spiritual independence.

The scholar was very careful about the source of gifts. He refused anything from the sultan or his affiliates, even reproaching his household if such gifts appeared. But he accepted gifts from those with lawful earnings, showing that his rejection was based on principle, not misanthropy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rejecting gifts
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the part about gifts from the sultan and the caliph's son.
Notice the contrast between rejecting gifts from rulers and accepting from others.
2
Identify the principles at play
Consider why he refused the caliph's son even when asked to give the gift in charity. What principle might have motivated this?
3
Reflect on the lesson
Think about how this principle of avoiding entanglements with worldly authorities can apply to spiritual life today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا سمع يوماً بوليمة أحد أبناء الدنيا تخلف من الحضور؛ خيفة أن يُدعى، فلا يظهرُ بالكلية حتى تمر أيام الوليمة وربما تخلف قبله أياماً. وحاشى نفسه عن قبول عطايا السلطان ومن لاذ به، وربما تأتي داره وهو غائب، فإذا وجدها أنكر على أهل داره، وتغيّر كثيراً، وكان يقبل هدايا غيرهم ممَّن طابت أكسابهم، ويدعو لهم. وقد جاءه يوماً ابن الخليفة ومعه عبدٌ، فأقبل يقبل يديه ورجليه، وطلب منه قبوله هدية، فأبى، ثم تبسم مطيِّباً قلبه ودعا له، فطلب منه أن يأخذه ويتصدق به على الفقراء، فأبى ولم يرضَ لهم ذلك. ولعله كان يُقصد بشفاعات وقضاء حوائج، ويُطلب منه الكتابة إلى الأمراء، فكان يكره ذلك، وإن فعل فعن حياء؛ فقد كلَّفه إنسان يوماً بكَتْبِ ثلاثين براءة، فكتبها وقال: هذه مصائب ابتلينا بها. وكان يتمنَّى ألا يرى أحداً وألا يراه أحدٌ، وكان يقول: لا حاجة لي بأحد ولا بماله. وأما عبادته وتبتله وخوفه من الله تعالى، وحسن معاملته مع عباده: فكان في هذا إمام زمانه، وقد أثرت فيه صحبة الصوفية وعلماء الحقائق، فطاب خبره ومخبره، وحَسُنت سيرته وسريرته. كان رضي الله عنه طويل الحزن، كثير الخوف، ولشدة خوفه يسمع لصدره أنين وهو مستغرق في الذكر، فلا يشعر بمن معه.
And if he heard one day of a banquet of one of the worldly people, he would refrain from attending, fearing that he might be invited, and he would not appear at all until the days of the banquet passed, and perhaps he would stay away days before it. And he kept himself away from accepting the gifts of the sultan and those who took refuge with him, and perhaps something would come to his house while he was absent, and if he found it, he would reproach his household and change greatly. And he would accept gifts from others whose earnings were lawful, and he would pray for them. And one day the son of the caliph came to him with a slave, and he began kissing his hands and feet, and asked him to accept a gift, but he refused; then he smiled, comforting his heart, and prayed for him. Then he asked him to take it and give it in charity to the poor, but he refused and was not pleased with that for them. And perhaps he was sought for intercessions and the fulfillment of needs, and was asked to write to the amirs, but he disliked that, and if he did it, it was out of modesty. For one day a man tasked him with writing thirty letters of recommendation, so he wrote them and said: 'These are calamities with which we are afflicted.' And he wished that no one would see him and he would see no one, and he would say: 'I have no need of anyone or his wealth.' And as for his worship, devotion, fear of God Almighty, and good treatment of His servants, he was in this the imam of his time. The companionship of the Sufis and the scholars of realities affected him, so his outward and inward were good, and his conduct and inner state were beautiful. He was, may God be pleased with him, of prolonged sorrow, much fear, and due to the intensity of his fear, a moaning would be heard from his chest while he was absorbed in dhikr, and he would not notice those with him.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage continues the biographical account, focusing on the scholar's renunciation of worldly possessions and his intense piety. It first describes how he avoided worldly banquets, even staying away for days to ensure he would not be invited. He refused gifts from the sultan or his affiliates, and would even reproach his family if such gifts appeared at home. However, he accepted gifts from those with lawful earnings, showing that his rejection was not about the gifts themselves but about the source. The anecdote about the caliph's son illustrates his firmness: despite being offered a slave, he refused even when asked to give it in charity, perhaps to avoid any sense of obligation. He disliked being sought for intercession or writing to authorities, and when compelled, he called it a 'calamity.' His wish to be unseen and his statement of self-sufficiency underscore his detachment. The passage concludes by praising his worship, fear of God, and good character, attributing his spiritual state to the influence of Sufis and scholars of realities. His constant sorrow, fear, and absorption in dhikr to the point of being unaware of others exemplify his deep devotion.

Key Terms
الهدية (gift)
السلطان (sultan)
العطايا (gifts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The scholar refused all gifts out of misanthropy or dislike of people.
Correction: He accepted gifts from those with lawful earnings, showing his rejection was based on religious scrupulousness about the source, not a general dislike of people.

Checkpoint
What did the scholar do when the son of the caliph offered him a slave as a gift?
A) He accepted it and thanked him.
B) He refused and then refused even to give it in charity.
C) He accepted and then gave it in charity.
D) He took it but later returned it.

Key Takeaway
The scholar's rejection of gifts from rulers was a deliberate spiritual practice to avoid dependence on worldly authorities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Whose gifts or favors do you currently accept that might be pulling your heart away from complete reliance on Allah? Will you examine your sources and cut the attachments that compromise your spiritual independence?
?
Al-Sanusi rejected even the opportunity to give a ruler's gift in charity. Are you holding on to something from a tainted source, thinking you can purify it by giving it away? What would it cost you to let go entirely?
?
If you were in al-Sanusi's position, would you have the courage to refuse the caliph's son—even when he kissed your hands? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to status and approval?

Avoiding Intercession and Writing to Authorities
Anecdote about disliking intercessions and writing to authorities, with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholar's reluctance to intercede or write to authorities, and his perspective on such requests as calamities.

Core Idea
He considered intercessions and writing to authorities as calamities to be avoided.

People often sought the scholar's help in interceding with rulers or writing letters of recommendation. He disliked this so much that he called it a 'calamity.' Yet, if compelled by modesty, he would comply while expressing his displeasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on intercession
Read the Arabic and translation about being sought for intercessions and writing to amirs.
Note his strong language: 'these are calamities'.
2
Analyze his attitude
Consider why he disliked intercession. Was it to avoid worldly involvement or something else?
3
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how a seeker of truth might balance helping others with maintaining spiritual detachment.

His Worship, Fear, and Devotion
Descriptive passage praising his worship, fear of God, and devotion.

Learning Objective

He was an imam of his time in worship, fear, and devotion.

The Saint's Asceticism: Refusing Gifts and Intercession
Describes the saint's refusal of gifts from worldly authorities and his desire for solitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the saint's deep asceticism and his reasons for refusing gifts and worldly intercession, as demonstrated through specific anecdotes.

Core Idea
True piety avoids even well-intentioned worldly attachments.

This passage gives us a window into the saint's character through two key practices: his careful acceptance of gifts only from pure sources, and his strong dislike for interceding with authorities. Through stories like the caliph's son and the thirty exemptions, we see a man who prized solitude and spiritual purity over social influence. Let's read his own words and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the sensitivity of his approach.
The language is straightforward but rich in meaning.
2
Understand his criterion for accepting gifts
He accepted gifts only from those with pure earnings. This shows his carefulness about the source of money.
3
Reflect on the incident with the caliph's son
Note his firm refusal, but also his kindness in smiling and praying for him. This is not harshness but piety.
He even refused to use the gift for charity.
4
Examine his dislike for intercession
He disliked writing to governors or interceding, and when he did it was out of shyness. The 'thirty exemptions' incident shows his discomfort.
He called such requests 'calamities'.
5
Consider his wish for solitude and the poem
He wished to be unseen and not to see others, and the poem praises his resistance to worldly allure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقبل هدايا غيرهم ممَّن طابت أكسابهم، ويدعو لهم. وقد جاءه يوماً ابن الخليفة ومعه عبدٌ، فأقبل يقبل يديه ورجليه، وطلب منه قبوله هدية، فأبى، ثم تبسم مطيِّباً قلبه ودعا له، فطلب منه أن يأخذه ويتصدق به على الفقراء، فأبى ولم يرضَ لهم ذلك. ولعله كان يُقصد بشفاعات وقضاء حوائج، ويُطلب منه الكتابة إلى الأمراء، فكان يكره ذلك، وإن فعل فعن حياء؛ فقد كلَّفه إنسان يوماً بكَتْبِ ثلاثين براءة، فكتبها وقال: هذه مصائب ابتلينا بها. وكان يتمنَّى ألا يرى أحداً وألا يراه أحدٌ، وكان يقول: لا حاجة لي بأحد ولا بماله (۱)، وقد قال فيه العلامة الحوضي (۲): ( من الكامل ) كم جاءَتِ الدنيا تسوقُ رئاسة يبغي إليك تقـربـاً أبناؤهــا فأبيت عنها معرضاً مستحقراً لم يخدعَنْكَ جمالها وبهاؤها.
He accepted gifts from others whose earnings were pure, and prayed for them. One day, the son of the caliph came to him with a slave, and began kissing his hands and feet, and asked him to accept a gift, but he refused. Then he smiled, comforting his heart, and prayed for him. He asked him to take it and give charity to the poor, but he refused and did not approve that for them. Perhaps he was sought for intercession and fulfilling needs, and was asked to write to the governors, and he disliked that, and if he did it, it was out of shyness. Once a person asked him to write thirty exemptions, and he wrote them and said: These are calamities we have been tested with. He used to wish not to see anyone and for no one to see him, and he used to say: I have no need of anyone or their wealth (1). And the scholar al-Hawdi said about him (2): (Al-Kamil meter) How many times did the world come offering leadership, its sons seeking nearness to you, But you turned away from it, disdainful, its beauty and splendor did not deceive you.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account describing the saint's attitude towards worldly gifts and intercession. It begins by noting that he accepted gifts only from those with pure earnings, indicating his carefulness about the source of money. Then it narrates an incident where the caliph's son came with a slave and tried to give a gift; he refused, but he was kind and prayed for him. When asked to give it in charity, he also refused. This shows his strictness in not handling wealth from authorities. The passage further explains that he disliked being sought for intercession or writing to governors; if he did it, it was out of shyness. An example is given: once someone asked him to write thirty 'براءة' (exemptions/free passes), and he wrote them but called them calamities. He wished for solitude and stated he had no need of anyone's wealth. The passage concludes with a poet's praise, likening him to resisting the world's allure. This narrative highlights his asceticism and avoidance of worldly entanglements.

Key Terms
زهد
تقوى
هدية
شفاعة
براءة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The saint refused gifts because he disliked people or had disdain for them.
Correction: He refused out of piety and avoidance of worldly attachments, not disdain. He smiled and prayed for the caliph's son, showing kindness.

Checkpoint
Why did the saint refuse to accept the gift from the caliph's son?
A) Because he disliked the caliph.
B) Because he feared the gift would distract him from worship.
C) Because he wanted to avoid worldly attachments and maintain piety.
D) Because the gift was impure.

Key Takeaway
The saint's rejection of gifts and intercession was motivated by a desire for spiritual purity and detachment from worldly influence, not by disdain for people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The saint turned away from leadership and gifts from the powerful. What gift or position are you currently pursuing that is pulling your heart away from Allah? Will you today make a decision to let it go?
?
He said, 'I have no need of anyone or their wealth.' Examine your heart: do you secretly depend on someone's wealth or influence for your security? How can you begin to truly depend only on Allah?
?
He called writing to governors a 'calamity.' In what ways are you entangled with worldly authorities for your own benefit? Are you willing to see those entanglements as calamities and seek to untangle yourself?

Details the saint's worship, devotion, and constant fear of God.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The saint's fear of God was so intense that his body involuntarily moaned during dhikr.

This passage from a classical biography provides a vivid glimpse into the inner state of a saint. It describes not only his outward acts of worship but the profound emotional and physical effects of his fear of God. The author credits the company of Sufis and scholars of realities for shaping this saint's character.

وأما عبادته وتبتله وخوفه من الله تعالى، وحسن معاملته مع عباده: فكان في هذا إمام زمانه، وقد أثرت فيه صحبة الصوفية وعلماء الحقائق، فطاب خبره ومخبره، وحَسُنت سيرته وسريرته. كان رضي الله عنه طويل الحزن، كثير الخوف، ولشدة خوفه يسمع لصدره أنين وهو مستغرق في الذكر، فلا يشعر بمن معه (۱).

May God be pleased with him. He was long in sorrow, abundant in fear, and due to the intensity of his fear, a moaning could be heard from his chest while he was immersed in dhikr, so that he did not perceive those with him (1).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage describes the saint's spiritual state. It begins by classifying his attributes into worship (عبادة), devotion (تبتل), fear of God, and good treatment of others. It states that in these respects he was the imam (leader) of his era. The influence of Sufi companionship and scholars of realities (علماء الحقائق) is credited for his excellent character both outwardly and inwardly. Then it gives a specific detail: he was perpetually sorrowful and fearful. His fear was so intense that while engaged in dhikr (remembrance of God), a moan could be heard from his chest, indicating deep spiritual anguish, and he was oblivious to those around him. This portrays his complete absorption in worship and fear of God.

The Saint's Humility, Compassion, and Gentle Character
Recounts the saint's humility, compassion, and gentle treatment of all creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound humility and compassion of the saint as demonstrated by his character traits and his reaction to a slaughtered wolf.

Core Idea
True sainthood combines humility, compassion, and deep awareness of God.

This passage paints a vivid picture of a saint whose character was marked by extraordinary humility and compassion. It shows how his treatment of people, from children to the weak, and even his response to a slaughtered animal, reflected his profound connection to God. Let's explore these qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from general character traits to the specific story.
The passage uses descriptive language and contrasts to highlight virtue.
2
Identify the saint's virtues
List the specific attributes mentioned: humility, good character, tenderness, mercy, smiling, welcoming speech, gentleness, patience, generosity, compassion, faithfulness.
How do these traits fit together?
3
Reflect on the wolf incident
Read the story of the wolf. What does his weeping and exclamation reveal about his compassion and his understanding of God's power?
This is a powerful example of his mercy extending to all creatures.
4
Consider the balance of qualities
Notice that he was humble yet could silence opponents; he was gentle but had authority. How do humility and strength coexist?
This addresses a potential misunderstanding that humility is weakness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع تواضع وحسن خلق ورقة قلب، رحيماً متبسماً في وجه من لقيه، إقبال وحسن كلام، يتزاحم الأطفال على تقبيل أطرافه، ليناً هيناً حتى في مشيه، ما ترى أحسن خلقاً، ولا أوسع صدراً، ولا أكرم نفساً، ولا أعطف قلباً، ولا أحفظ عهداً .. منه؛ يوفّر الكبير، ويقف مع الصغير، ويتواضع للضعفاء، مُعظَّماً جانب النبوة في غاية، حتى ارتحل الناس إليه وتبرَّكوا به. لا يعارضه أحد إلا أفحمه. جمع له العلم والعمل والولاية إلى النهاية، مع شفقته على الخلق وقضاء حوائجهم عند السلطان، والصبر على إذايتهم، وضع له من القبول والهيبة والإجلال في القلوب ما لم ينله غيره من علماء عصره وزُمَّاده. وكان جل وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته وذكر أحوال الآخرة، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى(۱). ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه: أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض .. بكى وقال: لا إله إلا الله، أين الروح التي يجري بها ؟!(۲)
With humility, good character, and tenderness of heart, merciful, smiling in the face of whoever met him, with welcome and good speech. Children would crowd to kiss his limbs. He was gentle and easy even in his walking. You would not see anyone better in character, nor more broad-chested (patient), nor more generous in soul, nor more compassionate in heart, nor more faithful to covenant… than him. He honored the old, stood with the young, and humbled himself to the weak, magnifying the side of prophethood to the utmost, so that people traveled to him and sought blessings from him. No one opposed him but he silenced him. He combined knowledge, action, and sainthood to the utmost, along with his compassion for creation, fulfilling their needs with the sultan, and patience with their harm. And there was placed for him acceptance, awe, and veneration in hearts that none other than him attained from the scholars of his time and his contemporaries. And most of his preaching to people was about fear of the great Lord, monitoring Him, and mentioning the states of the Hereafter. Yet despite this, he would preach to everyone according to what suited his condition, and rarely would you see him except moving his lips with the remembrance of God (1). Among the manifestations of his compassion and fear of his Lord, Exalted is He: that a wolf passed by him being chased by a hunter and his dogs, so they trapped it and then slaughtered it. When he got to it and saw it lying on the ground, he cried and said: 'There is no god but God, where is the soul that flows through it?!' (2).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive description of the saint's character traits. It first lists attributes: humility, good character, tenderness of heart, mercy, smiling, welcoming speech. Children loved him. He was gentle in his walk. He had no peer in goodness. He honored elders, cared for the young, and humbled himself to the weak, while greatly revering prophethood. People sought him out for blessings. He could silence any opponent. He combined knowledge, action, and spiritual station. He was compassionate, interceded for people with the sultan, and bore harm patiently. God gave him acceptance and awe in hearts beyond his contemporaries. His preaching focused on fear of God and the Hereafter, but he tailored his advice to each person. He was constantly in dhikr. The passage concludes with a striking story: upon seeing a slaughtered wolf, he wept and exclaimed about the lost soul, showing his profound compassion for all creatures and his awareness of God's power over life.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: His humility might be mistaken for weakness.
Correction: He was firm in silencing opponents and had great awe, showing humility and authority coexist.

Checkpoint

A) He ignored it.
B) He praised the hunter.
C) He wept and exclaimed about the soul.

Key Takeaway
The saint's compassion was so profound that he wept for a slaughtered wolf, reflecting his deep awareness of God's sovereignty over all souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The saint wept for a slaughtered wolf, seeing in it a soul that had been returned to its Creator. When you encounter the death of any creature—an insect, a bird, a pet—do you pause to reflect on the soul that once animated it, or do you pass by unmoved? What would it take for your heart to feel the loss of even a single soul as he did?
?
He tailored his preaching to each person, knowing their state. When you give advice to a friend, family member, or even a stranger, do you first consider their unique situation, or do you offer generic words? How can you cultivate the sensitivity to meet others where they are, as this saint did?
?
He was rarely seen except moving his lips in remembrance of God. In your quiet moments—when driving, waiting, or before sleep—what fills your mind and tongue? Is it the remembrance of God, or are you occupied with distractions? What would it take for dhikr to become your constant companion?

This passage offers a vivid portrait of a saint whose character exemplified the highest Islamic virtues. This shows his profound empathy even for a wild animal and his awareness of the soul's departure.
